There are those who think that engaging with others is enough, for since he has the power to bring others to life ("bring others to life"), this is proof that he himself is already completely ready, "With himself he is already completely finished." But the truth is—as the Rebbe Rayatz demands in a *sicha*[48](#_ftn703)—that one must also not forget about oneself ("One must also not forget about oneself").

The *yetzer hara*, "the clever one" (as he is called in the language of the Rebbe Rayatz[49](#_ftn704))—comes to everyone according to his nature:

To those who by nature think about themselves, "self-centered," the *yetzer* argues that one must do the *avodah* with oneself, to refine his body and *nefesh habahamit*, and not think about others, "Near your own palace you did not allow etc.,"[50](#_ftn705) "his lost object and the lost object of... his lost object takes precedence."[51](#_ftn706)

To this it is said: "Is it not to share your bread with the hungry, and bring the poor who are cast out to your house; when you see the naked, cover him."

And to those who by nature are activists, the *yetzer* argues that in a state of *pikuach nefesh* one should not think about oneself, because *pikuach nefesh* overrides the entire Torah.

[And to such an extent that the *yetzer* can come to someone and argue that he should leave his store

#### 148

open on Shabbos, Heaven forbid, in order to give another dollar to *tzedakah* for Jews who are in the transit camps ("D.P. camps") in order to bring them to the Holy Land, for "whoever walks four cubits in the Land of Israel is promised that he is a son of the World to Come,"[52](#_ftn707) and especially if he "speaks the Holy Tongue,"[53](#_ftn708) "it is not at all relevant to say"...].

To this it is said: "And do not hide yourself from your own flesh," do not forget about yourself ("You should also not forget about yourself").

**L.** One must not forget about oneself. One must not forget that he himself also has flesh, "*from your own flesh* (do not hide yourself)" specifically, coarse and material flesh, which must be refined and purified.

—The Rebbe Rayatz told[54](#_ftn709) that his father, the Rebbe (Rashab) *n.e.*, once expressed himself regarding wearing *tzitzis* on fleshy skin: "I don't need your flesh?! Flesh I can get at the kosher butcher as much as I want!"—these words were said "to a living child," a child who grew up later to be the leader of the generation!

Flesh—requires soaking, salting, and rinsing.[55](#_ftn710) And their meaning in the *avodah* of man[56](#_ftn711):

Soaking in water—refers to words of Torah which "are likened to water,"[57](#_ftn712) and also to prayer, as it is written,[58](#_ftn713) "Pour out your heart like water."

Salting—removing the blood from the flesh, that is, removing the heat and boiling ("the *koch*") of the flesh (unlike the blood that is sprinkled on the altar, which is blood of holiness).

And rinsing—that the removal of the blood should not be noticeable, that is, that no *yeshus* (ego) should be added to him from the *avodah* of removing the heat and boiling, "He should not go around and show off," to say: "See, here is the blood that I removed from myself, and now I am a *tzaddik*, and also a *baal teshuvah*..." Therefore, there must be a rinsing ("washing off") after the salting.

*

**M.** It was discussed above[59](#_ftn714) that in the recent period the Rebbe Rayatz gave instructions and clarified several matters regarding conduct in the time that follows.

#### 149

In the *sicha* of 13 Tammuz of last year,[60](#_ftn715) the Rebbe Rayatz said: "Every *chossid* must say every day a chapter of *Tehillim* specifically so that the merit of the Rebbes should be drawn down to them and the revelation of light should be absorbed in them in an interior way."

And seemingly: The influence of the Rebbes has existed since the time of the Baal Shem Tov, and nevertheless, the instruction regarding saying a chapter of *Tehillim* for the drawing down of the merit of the Rebbes and the absorption of the revelation of light in an interior way was not given—until 12 Tammuz 5709!

And one may say that the intent of this was that every *chossid* should continue to say the chapter of the Rebbe corresponding to his age.[61](#_ftn716)

There are *chassidim* who used to say the chapter of the Rebbe (Psalm 70), but after 10 Shevat, doubts arose in them whether to continue or not. And now, after 12 Tammuz, the Rebbe's birthday, doubts arose in them whether to continue saying Psalm 70 or to begin Psalm 71—whether even after the passing it is relevant to add and grow in years.[62](#_ftn717)

This question the Rebbe clarified by saying on the last 13 Tammuz in his lifetime in this world, that every *chossid* must say every day a chapter of *Tehillim*, the chapter of the Rebbe, through which the merit of the Rebbe will be drawn down and the revelation of light will be absorbed in an interior way.

This instruction the Rebbe said on 13 Tammuz of last year, but, at the time not everyone knew this instruction, and also the transcript of the *sicha* was with individuals, while the masses did not know about it. And now it has been printed and publicized to all.

**N.** The words of the Rebbe Rayatz, the intent of which was to clarify and instruct regarding the time that follows (after his passing), were not said explicitly, and the reason for this—as it is written regarding Moshe, "In all My house he is trusted,"[63](#_ftn718) and it is explained in the books[64](#_ftn719) (that seemingly, what relevance does trust have when it is not relevant to take anything there) that the trust is expressed in that not everything one sees does one reveal ("Not everything that one sees does one reveal").

#### 150

There is a story about the Maggid, it seems [although I have not seen this story in Chabad books, nevertheless, the instruction of the Rebbe Rayatz in such cases is known for the benefit of learning in fear of Heaven[65](#_ftn720)], that once one of the students entered to take leave of him before his journey home, and the Maggid told the *chevraya* (group) to delay him from his journey, and they tried to delay him, and when it did not help, they told him that they were doing so according to the instruction of the Maggid ("It originates from the Maggid himself"). The *chossid* could not believe it, because the Maggid himself had taken leave of him. He did not want to ask the Maggid, and therefore he decided to enter once more to take leave of the Maggid. The Maggid took leave of him again, and as he left, "he berated them as a *chossid* can"... saying, "You see that the Maggid took leave of me a second time." Meanwhile, the Maggid returned and told the *chevraya* again to delay him from his journey, and he entered again to the Maggid to take leave of him, and again they delayed him, and so it was several times, until in the end he did not listen to them and went on his way, and upon arriving home he passed away. They entered afterwards to the Maggid and asked him, since he knew etc., why did he not tell him explicitly not to travel? And the answer to this—"In all My house he is trusted," not everything is permitted to reveal.[66](#_ftn721)

**O.** Once someone asked about the fact that in "HaKriah VeHaKedusha" the Rebbe wrote matters of the future—why did he write in hints and not openly, and why after a period of time did he stop saying matters of the future.

And the matter of this:

If it had been the Rebbe's desire to be publicized ("that one should stamp him") as a "knower of the future," he would have done so, but, this is not his matter at all,

—It was discussed once[67](#_ftn722) that for *chassidim* it does not matter at all if the Rebbe is a knower of the future, and it is not because of matters of this kind that the connection to the Rebbe is made, the main thing is that he is the Rebbe—

And therefore, he did not reveal matters of the future for no reason, for, "In all My house he is trusted," but rather, to a certain extent for the need of the matter of "immediately to *teshuvah*."

**P.** In general, in the recent period, the Rebbe revealed many matters, even though once they did not reveal so much.

#### 151

In the *sicha* of the festival of Pesach of last year,[68](#_ftn723) the Rebbe Rayatz said that he decided to reveal something that until now he had not revealed,[69](#_ftn724) and added, "Even though this concerns me, one must do this with *mesiras nefesh* (self-sacrifice)!"

And from this we see that for the sake of a matter that concerns the collective "he put himself on the line," "he put his trust on the line even!"...

**Q.** Last summer there was some noise ("a bit of a commotion") about a war, and many people were afraid to do business, etc. A wealthy Jew, who used to give a lot of money to the *tzedakah* fund, decided to clarify the Rebbe's opinion if there was reason to fear a war or not. This Jew had "breadth," since he was educated on the knees of America... but nevertheless, he did not want to ask the Rebbe himself, and therefore he telephoned me and asked me to ask the Rebbe if in his opinion there would be a war, for then, he would not do the business, etc.

Since I also wanted to know what the Rebbe would answer to this question, and on the other hand, since I am only a "conduit" to convey the question of so-and-so, there would be no place for *kapida* (strictness)—I entered to the Rebbe and asked his question in the name of so-and-so.

The Rebbe raised his eyes and looked at me, smiled, and said: "It does not look like a war."

A few days ago, a certain Jew reminded me of this story.[70](#_ftn725)

*

**R.** The Mitteler Rebbe explains in *Shaarei Orah*[71](#_ftn726) the difference between the two modes of *Geulah*, "in its time" or "I will hasten it"[72](#_ftn727)—which mode is better for the benefit of the *avodah* of clarifications in elevating the sparks of holiness.

#### 151a

However, in these days of ours—a long time after the words of the *Gemara*[73](#_ftn728) that already "all the ends have passed"—for both opinions ("in its time" or "I will hasten it"), the *Geulah* will be in the better way whenever it comes, without waiting at all.

And may it be His will that the blessing of *Birchas HaChodesh* "for salvation and consolation" be fulfilled, speedily in our days, truly.

**********

## B’s”D. Reshimos of Wednesday, Parshas Va’eschanan, 12 Menachem-Av, 5710*

#### 151b

Unedited

The matter depends on your will—for the Rebbe certainly wills it—and if you only desire it, the Rebbe will effectuate his will in this shlichus through you.

Recite the kapitel Tehillim for now—kapitel 71—as is the custom of all Chassidim[1](#_ftn729), so that what the Rebbe wants from us may be internalized within us.

And likewise, keep a maamar of the Rebbe with you, as well as a picture of him.

## Sicha of 20 Menachem-Av, 5710

#### 152

**A.** It is known (from the *maamarim* of Lag B’Omer[1]) that on the day of the *yahrzeit* each year, an *aliyas haneshamah* (ascent of the soul) takes place. [The beginning of this ascent is on the Shabbos preceding the *yahrzeit*, when it is customary to receive an *aliyah* to the Torah (and if possible, *maftir*)[2], but the primary ascent is on the day of the *yahrzeit* itself.] Even though many years may have passed since the *histalkus*, nevertheless, when the *yahrzeit* arrives year after year, a greater ascent occurs. Therefore, there are special customs for the day of the *yahrzeit* — to lead the *davening* from the *amud*, and to share a *dvar Torah* (especially one of the descendants of the departed[3]), including a *dvar Torah* from the departed himself[4].

I was asked to write a *siyum* on Maseches Kiddushin according to the *nigleh* (the revealed dimension of Torah), and I deferred it until the *yahrzeit*. And since those present here are people of Torah or *yeshivah bochurim* who are currently immersed in Torah, surely they will not mind ("*es vet zey nit harn*") if the time is extended.

And so that it should be with joy ("*es zol zeyn freylecher tzu hern*") — let us first say *l’chaim*.

*

**B.** At the end of Maseches Kiddushin, there is a statement brought in a *Baraisa*, and before that in the *Mishnah*. One must analyze and explain the differences and changes between the *Baraisa* and the *Mishnah*.

By way of introduction: In the general methods of study, there are several approaches. There is the method of logic and conceptualization — known as "*der Litvisher lernen*," and the method of *chidud* (sharpness) and *pilpul*, which is associated with Poland. However, precision in language and style is not commonly practiced.

The reason for this—presumably—is due to a lack of time. Another reason is the difference between the Written Torah and the Oral Torah: in the Written Torah, the primary focus is on the *letters*,

#### 153

for we find that for a single letter in the Torah, there is an entire chapter in the *Shas*[5], whereas in the Oral Torah, the primary focus is on the *concept*, as explained in *Likutei Torah*[6].

Nevertheless, it is obvious that even in the Oral Torah, the language is extremely precise, to the extent that from the precision of the language, one can derive mountains of concepts.

One of the great Chassidim of the Alter Rebbe, R' Nechemia of Dubrovna[7], would be precise even in the language of the *Taz* or the *Magen Avraham*, and from that he would derive a *halachic* ruling. How much more so must we be precise in the language of the *Shas*, as follows.

**C.** The text of the *siyum* of Maseches Kiddushin states:

"It was taught: Rabbi Nehorai says: I set aside every craft in the world and I teach my son only Torah, for every craft in the world stands by him only in the days of his youth, but in the days of his old age he is cast into hunger. But Torah is not so; it stands by a person at the time of his youth and gives him an end and a hope at the time of his old age. At the time of his youth, what does it say[8]: 'But they that wait for Hashem shall renew their strength; they shall mount up with wings as eagles.' In his old age, what does it say[9]: 'They shall still bring forth fruit in old age; they shall be full of sap and flourishing.'"

The aforementioned statement is also brought in the *Mishnah*, and at greater length. It states:

"Rabbi Nehorai says: I set aside every craft in the world and I teach my son only Torah, for a person eats from its reward in this world, and the principal remains for him in the World to Come. And all other crafts are not so; when a person comes to illness, or to old age, or to suffering, and he cannot engage in his work, he dies of hunger. But Torah is not so; rather, it guards him from all evil in his youth and gives him an end and a hope in his old age. In his youth, what does it say: 'But they that wait for Hashem shall renew their strength.' In his old age, what does it say: 'They shall still bring forth fruit in old age.' And so it says regarding Avraham Avinu[10]: 'And Avraham was old... and Hashem blessed Avraham in all.' We find that Avraham Avinu performed the entire Torah before it was given, as it is said[11]: 'Because Avraham hearkened to My voice and kept My charge, My commandments, My statutes, and My laws.'"

One must understand:

Regarding the content of the statement: It is stated in the *Tosefta* (brought in the first chapter[12]) that a person is obligated to teach his son a craft, and it implies that there is no dispute regarding this. If so, what is the meaning of his saying, "I set aside every craft in the world"[13]?

Regarding the difference[14] between the *Mishnah* and the *Baraisa*: It is known what the Rambam wrote in his introduction to the *Commentary on the Mishnah*[15], that Rabbi [Yehudah HaNasi] taught everything briefly, "a short matter that includes many concepts." Therefore, the *Mishnah* is brief. However, later, when intellectual capacity diminished, it became necessary to detail the many particulars. Therefore, the norm in *Shas* is that concepts are taught in the *Mishnah* briefly, and in the *Baraisa* at length and with detail. Based on this, the *style of the statement* mentioned above is very puzzling, for in the *Mishnah* it is at length and with detail, and in the *Baraisa* it is brief—the opposite of the order in the entire *Shas*!

It is also necessary to understand the reason for several changes in the *language of the statement* between the *Mishnah* and the *Baraisa*: (1) In the *Mishnah* the language is "*all other* crafts," and in the *Baraisa* "*every* craft in the world." (2) In the *Mishnah* it lists illness, old age, and suffering, and in the *Baraisa* only old age. (3) In the *Mishnah* the language is "*his youth* and his old age," and in the *Baraisa* "*his childhood* and his old age." (4) In the *Mishnah* it adds regarding Torah, "that a person eats... for the World to Come," and in the *Baraisa* it does not. (5) In the *Mishnah* it concludes, "And so it says regarding Avraham...," and in the *Baraisa* it does not.

**D.** Now, in general, regarding the methods of *kushya* (question) and *terutz* (resolution), even if at first glance the resolution seems correct, it is often necessary to reject it. Therefore, in *every* resolution there is room for doubt—perhaps it is not true, and with proper contemplation, a refutation will be found.

However, if through one premise and one resolution two or three questions on the subject are resolved—and all the more so when the questions are of a different nature—this strengthens the hypothesis that the resolution is true. As the number of questions resolved by *one* premise increases, the hypothesis approaches certainty.

And so it is in our case—that through one premise, one can resolve the differences between the *Mishnah* and the *Baraisa* and all the aforementioned questions, as follows.

**E.** This can be understood by first noting the well-known ruling of the Rambam[16] that forbids rabbis and Torah scholars from taking their sustenance from the community. He speaks at length against those who derive their livelihood from

#### 155

the Torah and make it a "spade with which to dig," using the crown of Torah (in the language of Chassidus, the *Chochmah* and Will of the Holy One, blessed be He). He brings many proofs for this from the *Gemara*. However, those who came after him disagreed with him and brought several proofs that it is permitted to derive sustenance from the Torah.

The conclusion of the Alter Rebbe[17] is: "They did not warn and prevent one from making it a 'spade' except so that the *beginning* of his study should not be for the sake of it becoming a craft to earn a living, for then he is using the crown of Torah for mundane use. But if he studied for the sake of Heaven, and afterwards he has no way to earn a living except through teaching or judging and giving rulings, he is using it *for its own need*. For if he does not have what to eat, he will not be able to engage in it properly while he is suffering from hunger, etc. And if he can engage in some work to support himself but sets aside his work to support himself from teaching Torah to students, all the more so is he using it *only for its own need*, because in order not to be idle *from Torah study*, he sets aside the work he could have engaged in to support himself."

Seemingly, the permission for this can be explained by two rationales: (a) The difference between *for his own need* and *for its need*—that it is forbidden to use it to make it a spade to dig with *for his own need*, whereas when he sets aside work he could have used to support himself and supports himself from Torah so as not to be idle from Torah study, he is using it *for its need*. (b) The difference between *l’chatchilah* (initially) and *b’dieved* (after the fact)—that *l’chatchilah* it is certainly forbidden to study in order to earn a living, but *b’dieved*, if he studied it permissibly, for its own sake, and afterwards he has no choice because he must support himself, then he is not obligated to *now* learn a craft, since he can support himself *immediately* from the Torah *that he studied permissibly*[18]. This is similar to the example that it is forbidden to set out on a ship or a caravan close to Shabbos if it will cause a violation of Shabbos, but if he set out earlier permissibly (three days before Shabbos), it is permitted to continue traveling with the ship or caravan, since he set out permissibly, and afterwards *pikuach nefesh* (saving a life) overrides Shabbos[19]. And so it is in the aforementioned matter.

A more exalted way is that he engages in Torah only, and yet he does not support himself from it, but his work is done by others, as the words of R' Shimon bar Yochai in *Gemara Berachos*[20]: "Is it possible that a person plows at the time of plowing and harvests at the time of harvesting, etc. (as the words of R' Yishmael that one must 'gather in your corn[21],' 'conduct yourself with them in the manner of the world')—what will become of Torah? Rather, at the time that Israel does the will of the Omnipresent, their work is done by others, as it is said[22]: 'And strangers shall stand and feed your flocks, etc.'" The *Gemara* concludes: "Many have done... like R' Shimon bar Yochai and it did not succeed for them," meaning, this is a path only for unique individuals like R' Shimon bar Yochai, and similarly R' Akiva (as it is in *Maseches Nedarim*[23]), and the like.

Thus, there are three ways in Torah study: (a) In order to support oneself from it—which is forbidden. (b) Study for its own sake, but afterwards, if he has no way to support himself, he supports himself from it—which is permitted. (c) To make his Torah study permanent and his work is done by others through a miracle—which is the path for unique individuals.

**F.** Based on this, we can understand the statement of R' Nehorai, "I set aside every craft in the world," even though a person is obligated to teach his son a craft—the meaning of this is that he will support himself in one of the aforementioned ways: from the Torah itself—in a permitted manner as the Alter Rebbe wrote, or that his work will be done by others.

With this, we can understand the precision of the language, "and I teach my *son* only Torah." Seemingly, it should have said "and I study only Torah." Why the precision of "son" specifically?

[Simply put, it could be said that this is because that is the language of the sections that preceded the statement of R' Nehorai, in which it is said "teach his *son*," from the beginning of the *Mishnah*: "A person should not teach his *son* the craft of women." And at the beginning it is precise, because for himself it is permitted, and specifically for his son, for whom the obligation of teaching is upon the father—meaning while he is a child, and consequently, while he is single—it is forbidden. Therefore, it concludes in the *Mishnah* in all the subsequent sections also regarding his son.]

The explanation of this is that for himself, it is certainly forbidden to say "I set aside every craft, etc.," for if so, he is making the Torah a spade with which to dig. This is not the case when he teaches his *son*, for there is no prohibition in the matter, for when he is a child, he does not need sustenance and he studies for its own sake, and afterwards this will have the status of *b’dieved*, as mentioned above.

And even though his father knows *l’chatchilah* that afterwards the son will be forced to do this, nevertheless it is permitted, similar to the fact that it is permitted to set out on a ship or caravan three days before Shabbos, even though it is known that afterwards he will be forced to travel on Shabbos, since currently it is permitted and afterwards it will be the law of *b’dieved*.

**G.** Seemingly, one could raise a question on this:

It is stated in the first chapter[24] that if he is to study and his son is *capable* of studying, his son takes precedence, whereas if he is to study and a *stranger* is capable of studying, he takes precedence. The reason for this distinction is that when his son

#### 157

studies Torah through his efforts, it is as if he himself is *studying*, whereas with a stranger[25]. Based on this, when he sets aside all crafts and teaches his son only Torah, the father is also studying, and the study is with the intent that they will afterwards make this Torah that he is studying a spade to dig with. If so, it is the forbidden way! For what difference does it make regarding the desecration—who is desecrating it by making it a spade afterwards, when at the beginning of the father's study, the father already knows he will make it a spade?

But—it is not so, for "follow the reasons": by what and when does the desecration occur—when they support themselves from the Torah. Consequently, we must only judge regarding the one who supports himself, which is the son, and the son initially studied permissibly for its own sake. *V’kal l’havin*.

**H.** Based on this, we can explain the content of the difference between the *Mishnah* and the *Baraisa*:

It was mentioned above that the *Mishnah* speaks of general matters and the *Baraisa* also of details. Also in our case—the *Mishnah* explains the path for Torah study that applies to the *general public*, meaning, the path to support oneself from the Torah itself in a permitted manner. In the *Baraisa*, it also explains the path that applies only to *unique individuals* like R' Shimon bar Yochai, which is the path where their work is done by others.

With this, the changes in language in the *Baraisa* and the *Mishnah* will be explained:

The language of the *Baraisa*: "*every* craft in the world does not stand by him, etc.," and the language of the *Mishnah*: "*all other* crafts, etc.":

The difference between "all" and "other" is simple: the meaning of "*every* craft in the world" is that the Torah is *not* included among them, and it distinguishes between Torah and craft, that all crafts do not stand by him, whereas Torah does. The meaning of "*all other* crafts" is that the Torah is also included among the crafts, but it distinguishes between the craft of Torah and other crafts.

The explanation for this is that the *Mishnah* speaks about the path that applies to everyone, meaning that he supports himself as a teacher of rulings, etc., and then the Torah is also a craft, for he supports himself from it. This is not the case in the *Baraisa*, which explains the path of R' Shimon bar Yochai where his work is done by others, for then the Torah is not a craft at all.

Based on this, another small change will be sweetened: in the *Baraisa* it says every craft *in the world*, and this word is not in the *Mishnah*—because, in the *Baraisa*, the conduct of Torah is not in the manner of "the way of the world," but the *opposite* of every craft in the world. This is not the case in the *Mishnah*, where he supports himself from the Torah not through miraculous conduct.

#### 158

And so it is in the continuation of the *Mishnah*, that all other crafts, etc., when a person comes to illness or old age or suffering, he dies of hunger, and in the *Baraisa* it omitted this and brought only old age:

Our Sages said there is no death without sin and no suffering without iniquity, etc., except for exceptions that are not common[26]. Therefore, in the *Baraisa*, which speaks about conduct similar to R' Shimon bar Yochai and his colleagues, where Israel does the will of the Omnipresent, "with all your might[27]," the first paragraph of *Krias Shema* in which it is not said "Take heed to yourselves, lest your heart be deceived, etc.[28]," and as our Sages said in *Berachos* 21, then the matter of illness and suffering is not applicable, only natural old age.

Based on this, it is also understood that in the *Mishnah* it says *ne'arut* (youth), and not *yaldut* (childhood), which is younger than *ne'arut*[29] [and above in our *masechta*[30]: "Train the *na'ar* (youth)[31] (and what is his *ne'arut*, Rashi explains) until 22, until 24 years old"], and in the *Baraisa* it says *yaldut*—because, in the conduct of "the way of the world" where he supports himself from Torah through teaching and rulings, etc. (in the *Mishnah*), he must be at least somewhat older and sharp (*ne'arut*). This is not the case in the conduct where his work is done by others (in the *Baraisa*), since he does the will of the Omnipresent, this can be even in his *yaldut* (which is not literally childhood, but similar to the language in *Sukkah*[32], "Happy is our *yaldut* (childhood), etc.").

**I.** Based on this, the reason is understood why only in the *Mishnah* it says regarding Torah that a person eats from its reward in this world, and not in the *Baraisa*:

By way of introduction, the difference between the two aforementioned permitted ways of sustenance—that in the way of conduct like R' Shimon bar Yochai where his work is done by others, this is not because of the merit of *Torah* specifically, that they eat from its reward in this world, but because they do the will of the Omnipresent, "with all your might," the reward for which is "And strangers shall stand and feed your flocks," and in a way that happens by itself. Since he has no worries of sustenance, he is obligated in Torah study constantly. What is said in the *Baraisa*, "But Torah is not so," the emphasis is not on *Torah*, but that through this it is proven that he is one who "waits for Hashem" and consequently "shall renew their strength, etc." This is not the case in the second way, where the world follows its custom in the manner of "the way of the world," he is assured of his sustenance because of the *merit of Torah* specifically, that they eat from its reward in this world as well.

With this, it is also understood why in the *Mishnah* it brought the verse regarding Avraham being old, and in the *Baraisa* it omitted it—because:

#### 159

Avraham was a shepherd, meaning his sustenance was according to nature. And even though he had success above the way of nature, nevertheless, this is not the matter of it being done by others, like R' Shimon bar Yochai and R' Akiva mentioned above and the like, for Avraham engaged in his work, only that in his work itself he had success that was above the way of nature. And even though Avraham had servants and he himself engaged in Torah and *avodah*, and seemingly this is the matter of his work being done by others, as it says "And strangers shall stand and feed your flocks," in Avraham this was also through his own work. Therefore, the Patriarchs chose to be shepherds specifically so that they would not have *great* worry in their sustenance, as explained in *Chassidic* texts[33], which itself proves that there was some participation on their part, and not that their work was done by others, and it is the natural way as mentioned above.

Therefore, it omitted in the *Baraisa* the matter of Avraham, because there is no proof from this that one can support himself through *miraculous* deeds. But in the *Mishnah*, which speaks of sustenance in the natural way, it brought the verse of Avraham, that through his keeping the entire Torah, even when he was old he was blessed with "all," success in nature.

*

**J.** Now, the difference between the *Mishnah* and the *Baraisa* is also in the inner dimensions of the matters [as in all matters in Creation, and especially in matters of Torah and *Mitzvos*, when there are differences in the revealed dimension of things, it is because that is how it is in the inner dimension]—and in any case, in brief:

There are several levels in Torah from the perspective of *itself*. And they are generally two: the inner dimension of Torah, regarding which it is said[34] "And I was with Him," with Him specifically, for in this level, all the worlds *and their conduct* are not considered (not even as nothing) at all[35]. And the external dimension of Torah—which is connected with the worlds and their vitality.

And this difference is also in Torah *from the perspective of man's service*—for the inner dimension of Torah is called the *Tree of Life*[36], and it is above the worlds and their conduct, which are mixed with good and evil. And the revealed dimension of Torah, which is called the *Tree of Good and Evil*[36], is so named because it is clothed in matters of good and evil, false claims, etc.[37]

And to the second category mentioned above (*Tree of Good and Evil*) belongs the study explained in the *Mishnah*, and then it is possible to say illness[38], etc. And to the first category (*Tree of Life*) is the conduct in the *Baraisa* that "strangers shall stand and feed your flocks," since they do the will (the will that is above the *Seder Hishtalshelus*, and this they draw down until it becomes His will) of the Omnipresent[39].

**K.** One should add to this based on what our Sages said[40]: "We find that the Holy One, blessed be He, forgave [the sins of] idolatry, forbidden relationships, and bloodshed, but He did not forgive the despising of the Torah (neglect of Torah study)... Would that they had abandoned Me but kept My Torah, for through their engaging in it, *the light* within it would have returned them to the good"—"*the light* (ha-ma'or) within it" specifically, which refers to the inner dimension of Torah[41]:

By way of introduction, as explained in *Kuntres Eitz Chaim*[42] in the interpretation of the verse[43] "For the commandment is a lamp, and the Torah is light, and the way of life, etc."—that seemingly, "what is the matter of 'way of life,' what is the way of life besides the Torah and the commandment mentioned before"—that "Torah is light" refers to the revealed dimension of Torah, and "way of life" refers to the inner dimension of Torah.

And this is also the difference between *ohr* (light) and *ma'or* (the source of light)—that the revealed dimension of Torah is called *ohr* ("Torah is light") and the inner dimension of Torah is called *ma'or*:

*Ohr*—as it moves away from its source, it diminishes, until it is possible for there to be an interruption, at which point it is nullified entirely. This is not the case with *ma'or*, which is always present.

An example of this in spirituality is the difference between the revealed dimension of Torah and the inner dimension of Torah—that through the study of the revealed dimension of Torah alone, which is compared to *ohr*, there can be an interruption, *chas v'shalom*, through sins, as it is said[44] "Your iniquities have separated, etc.," until he can stumble in the three aforementioned things—in subtlety at least (as explained in *Tanya*[45] that through a sin one becomes separated from His unity, may He be blessed, like in idolatry, and especially in those sins regarding which our Sages said it is as if he is worshipping idols, as explained in *Iggeres HaTeshuvah*[46]). This is not the case through the study of the inner dimension of Torah, the "*ma'or* within the Torah," for even when he stumbles in the three aforementioned things, "the *ma'or* within it returns him to the good."

And one can connect this with the words of the *Gemara*[47]: "'Torah commanded us Moshe[48],' *Torah* in *gematria* is 611 (and this is what is written: *Torah* (611) commanded us *Moshe*, and two) 'I am' and 'You shall have no other gods'[49] *from the mouth of the Almighty* we heard them"—that even when one lacks, *chas v'shalom*, in the matter of "I am" and "You shall have no other gods," the matter of faith, this is rectified through the study of Torah ("Torah" in *gematria* is 611, excluding the two of "I am" and "You shall have no other gods"), since "*the ma'or* within it returns him to the good."

**L.** And this is also the content of the statement of Rabbi *Nehorai*: "I set aside every craft in the world and I teach my son only Torah":

By way of introduction, the explanation of the two ways of the *Mishnah* and the *Baraisa*, that the difference between them is whether Torah is included in "craft" or is above it (as mentioned above)—based on what our Sages said[50]: "*Initially*, when a person acts, he acts *for his own sake*," for the soul descended only to rectify the body and the animal soul and its portion in the world[51], which is *the craft* of Torah study "for his own sake." And afterwards, he comes to the level of study *for its own sake*, for the sake of the Torah, above every craft in the world, including also the craft of rectifying the body and the world.

And regarding this, Rabbi Nehorai says: "I set aside every craft in the world and I teach my son only Torah... (which) stands by a person at the time of his youth and at the time of his old age"—the Torah of Rabbi *Nehorai* specifically, for he "illuminates the eyes of the wise[52]," which is the matter of the "*ma'or* within the Torah"—because:

In the level of Torah that is a craft to rectify the body and the world, because it is through a war with an opponent, "and one nation shall be stronger than the other nation[53]," it is possible for there to be an interruption *for a time* (for in the end he will be victorious). This is not the case in the level of Torah that is above the craft of rectifying the body and the world, Torah for its own sake, where an interruption is not possible. Rather, "it stands by a person at the time of his youth and at the time of his old age," and more than that, not only in a state of old age, general weakness, but even in a state of suffering (which can also be because of the state of his household or his environment), and even to a state of illness (where he himself is ill), where he lacks in the fulfillment of the 248 positive commandments and 365 negative commandments which correspond to the 248 limbs and 365 sinews[54]—even in such a state, "*the ma'or* within it returns him to the good."

*

**M.** The above is also regarding the study of the *Chassidic* teachings of the Rebbe:

By way of introduction: in previous generations, such as in the time of the Rambam, there was great caution not to reveal the teachings of Kabbalah (as explained in the *sichos* of the Rebbe[55]), and even in the time of the Arizal (who said that "it is permitted and a *mitzvah* to reveal this wisdom"[56]), they required several preparations for the study of Kabbalah[57] (such as saying *Yehi Ratzon*). And even the Baal Shem Tov commanded not to study books of Kabbalah for one who does not know how to strip the matters of their physicality (as the Tzemach Tzedek brings[58]), and the story is also known regarding the strictness regarding the Maggid that the Alter Rebbe saved[59].

And even after the Alter Rebbe and our Rebbes who followed him "poured it out," nevertheless, in normal years, they needed "entire preparations" to study *Chassidus* or to enter *yechidus*, as the Rebbe told about Chassidim who prepared for *yechidus* for several years[60].

Based on this, when one contemplates his state *aliba d'nafshi* (according to himself), he might think that he does not belong to the study of *Chassidus*, and especially to influence others.

The answer to this is that today there is no time to postpone the study of *Chassidus* until the preparations are finished. One must study the *Chassidus* of the Rebbe, return and study, "*veyter lernen un vidder lernen*," "hold on to the Rebbe's door handle," and "*the ma'or* within it"—the essence of the Rebbe that he placed into his teachings—"returns him to the good," "the Rebbe will already pull him out of all the mud."

And not only that, but even if he is in a standing and state where he still has doubts regarding the Rebbe (if he is the master of everything, and can do everything, and consequently he cannot hide from him), he must study the *Chassidic* teachings of the Rebbe—"Torah commanded us Moshe," even though "Torah" in *gematria* is 611, meaning, that he still lacks in the matter of "I am" and "You shall have no other gods" (as mentioned above in section K), and an example of this regarding the Rebbe, as understood from our Sages (according to *nigleh*): "He who disputes his teacher is as one who disputes the *Shechinah*[61]"—for, through studying his teachings, "he will hold on to the Rebbe's door handle and be with him on one wagon."

**N.** And to add, that the need for the study of his teachings, "Torah commanded us Moshe," is emphasized most after the *histalkus*:

The saying that the Rebbe (Rashab) said before his *histalkus* is known: "I am going to heaven and the writings I leave for you[62]." The explanation for this—seemingly, what does he teach us, for it is self-evident that upon his ascent to heaven the writings remain in the same place they were initially placed—is that even after he ascends to heaven, his essence remains in the writings that he left for us, and through the study of the writings "one has *him*," and not only as he was below, but also as he is in his ascent above [as explained in the *maamarim* of R' Hillel (which are now in print)[63] that at the time of the *histalkus* he sheds the garments of *Beriah, Yetzirah, Asiyah*, and at the time of the *hishtatchus* (visiting the gravesite) he sheds also the garments of *Atzilus*], "one has him" in his ascent above—through the (study of the) writings.

And this matter is hinted at in "Torah commanded us Moshe"—"*tzivah*" (commanded) from the language of *tzavta* (companionship) and connection[64]—that through his Torah, he (Moshe) connects with us, and consequently, we are connected with him in all his ascents ("*vi hoych er is*"), since he placed his essence into his Torah.

**O.** And the *Chassidic* teachings of the Rebbe, the *ma'or* within the Torah, stands by him at the time of his youth and at the time of his old age, and also in a state of suffering and illness:

Illness, which is the matter of a lack in the 248 positive commandments and 365 negative commandments that correspond to the 248 limbs and 365 sinews—includes also a lack in the fulfillment of the command and order of the Rebbe (and also his request, as he once said: "My request is a command"), which one is obligated to fulfill like all the commandments of the Sages whose obligation is learned from "You shall not turn aside from the way which they shall tell you[65]," or from "Ask your father and he will tell you[66]" (as there are two opinions on this[67]). And not only that, but according to the Rambam[68], one who transgresses the command of the Sanhedrin ("who are the pillars of instruction and from them law and judgment go out to all Israel") who enacted a decree and the matter spread throughout all Israel, transgresses a positive commandment and a negative commandment *from the Torah*. And so it is for Chassidim regarding the fulfillment of the command and order of the Rebbe in the manner of "You shall not turn aside, etc.," since the Rebbe is the pillar of instruction and from him law and judgment go out to all his students who are his Chassidim, and when they transgress a command and order of the Rebbe, it is as if they are transgressing a positive commandment and a negative commandment[69], which is the matter of illness.

The advice for this—to study the *Chassidic* teachings of the Rebbe, which stands by him even in a state of illness, to fulfill the command and order and will of the Rebbe, and to walk in his ways

## With the help of Hashem. Sicha of Shabbos Parshas Re’eh, Mevarchim Chodesh Elul, Erev Rosh Chodesh Elul, 5710

#### 174

**A.** On this Shabbos, Shabbos Mevarchim the month of Elul, we read in most years (as in this year) Parshas Re’eh (except when Rosh Chodesh Elul falls on Shabbos, in which case we read Parshas Re’eh on Shabbos Rosh Chodesh Elul). We must explain the connection and relevance of Parshas Re’eh to the avodah of the month of Elul.

Among the many laws stated in Parshas Re’eh is the law [1] regarding the signs of purity in domesticated and wild animals: "This [2] is the animal that you may eat, etc." (it also counts wild animals, from which "we learn that the wild animal is included in the category of the domesticated animal" [3]), "Every [4] animal that has a split hoof and cloven hooves, etc." ("divided into two claws" [5], "separated above and below into two claws" [6]) and chews its cud, etc." (and afterwards it lists those that have only one sign, which are forbidden to be eaten).

Regarding the signs, there is an inquiry [7] whether the meaning is that because it has a split hoof and chews its cud, therefore it is pure—meaning that the signs are the *cause* of the purity—or whether the purity is due to other reasons, and Hashem established these signs in them *to clarify* the purity so that *we would know* which are the pure animals.

There are several proofs that the signs are the *cause* of the purity. This is also implied by the language of the verse regarding those that have only one sign: "*For* it chews the cud, but it does not split the hoof (and therefore) it is impure to you, etc. *For* it splits the hoof but does not chew the cud (and therefore) it is impure to you" [8]—meaning that the sign or its absence is the cause of the purity or impurity.

It should be noted that the Tziyuni at the beginning of Parshas Shemini writes that "the signs that were explained in the Torah... their existence is not the cause of the permission, nor is their absence the cause of the prohibition;

#### 175

rather, they are signs by which one knows the appropriate species from the inappropriate." However, from the continuation of his words in his explanation according to Kabbalah, it is implied that the signs cause the purity or impurity, as the language of the verse implies.

[In any case, even if we say that the signs are only for clarification, it is necessary to say that they belong to the purity (and are not entirely peripheral matters), coming as a result and consequence of it; for "everything that Hashem created in His world, He did not create one thing in vain" [9]. Why would it be necessary to create special signs that do not belong to the purity, solely to clarify the purity, at a time when they could clarify the purity through signs that do belong to the purity—the cause of the purity or its consequence?]

We must explain the lesson and instruction for a person’s avodah regarding the "animal" within him—the *nefesh habehamis*—which he must refine and transform into holiness so that it becomes a pure animal. This matter is especially relevant to the avodah of the month of Elul, as will be explained.

**B.** By way of introduction to the explanation of the nature of the month of Elul:

The Rebbe (Rayatz) explains [10] that the month of Elul is the month of *cheshbon hanefesh* (soul-searching). This is analogous to a business owner who, from time to time, must make a general accounting of what is happening in his business, which leads to proper conduct and success in business. So too in avodah: there is a special time of the year, the month of Elul, when one must make a general accounting of his standing and situation in the service of Hashem, in the fulfillment of Torah and mitzvos, and in conduct with good character traits.

The time for *cheshbon hanefesh* is in the month of Elul, whereas throughout the rest of the year one must occupy himself with Torah and avodah without thinking about his general standing and situation. Like a business owner who only makes a *general* accounting of the business's status from time to time, but most of the time is occupied with the commerce itself—at which point he only makes an accounting regarding the *specific* matter he is about to do, whether it is worthwhile to do it and how to do it—but an accounting of his general status he only does from time to time. For if he were to make a general accounting every day, he would never get to the business itself as required. So too in avodah: all year long one must occupy himself with Torah and avodah, including making a *specific* accounting during the *krias shema she'al hamitah* of every day regarding that day's avodah (and similarly before Shabbos regarding the week's avodah, and before Rosh Chodesh regarding the whole month). However, the *general* accounting of his overall standing and situation in the service of Hashem is only in the month of Elul. For if he were to make a

#### 176

general accounting every day, he would be occupied most of the day (three-quarters of the day [11]) with making calculations, and he would have no time left to occupy himself with Torah and avodah.

**C.** Someone might argue: An accounting is a good thing, so why not make a general accounting every day?

Furthermore—he continues to argue—how can he occupy himself with Torah and avodah before making an accounting? After all, the verse explicitly says [12], "But to the wicked, G-d says, 'What right have you to recite My statutes?'" And it is explained in Tanya [13] that "it is impossible for the wicked to begin to serve Hashem without first doing teshuvah for the past." Therefore, he must make an accounting first to know what he needs to rectify. Furthermore, even after teshuvah, it must be that "my sin is *always* before me" [14] [15], as explained in Tanya [16].

The answer to this is that one must know that this is a *tactic of the yetzer hara*—that he should occupy himself with making calculations instead of occupying himself with Torah and avodah. One must occupy himself with Torah and avodah without making calculations, and when the month of Elul arrives, then he should make the calculations.

It is brought in the sichos of the Rebbe (Rashab) [17] that someone cried on Simchas Torah, and the Rebbe told him that since he did not cry during the recitation of "Al Chet" on Yom Kippur, he is completing it now, but this is *out of its proper time*.

"G-d made man upright" [18], and He gave a specific time for everything. In our case, the time for making an accounting is the month of Elul.

**D.** Among the reasons why the time for the accounting is specifically in the month of Elul is that the Thirteen Attributes of Mercy shine in it [19]:

Making a true accounting of one's soul can sometimes cause harm. For when one knows his standing and situation—and especially since even "the heavens [20] are not pure in His eyes," etc.—he is liable to fall into despair and think that since the situation is lost anyway, what is the benefit of his exertion, etc.?

#### 177

Therefore, they postpone the making of the accounting to the month of Elul, in which the Thirteen Attributes of Mercy shine: "Slow to anger, etc., forgiving iniquity, transgression, and sin, and cleansing" [21].

—And especially according to what is explained [19] that the revelation of the Thirteen Attributes of Mercy in the month of Elul is like the parable of the King in the field (unlike the revelation of the Thirteen Attributes of Mercy on Rosh Hashanah and the Ten Days of Repentance, which is like the parable of the King sitting in His royal palace). The King goes out to the people who are in the field, which is not a place of human habitation. The parallel is that even when one is in the place and level of a "field" due to the undesirable matters (which are not done in a place of human habitation) that were done throughout the year, there is a revelation of the Thirteen Attributes of Mercy—

And through this, he can make an accounting without despairing of his standing and situation, knowing that regardless of his standing and situation in the past year, he can return and draw close to Hashem and occupy himself with Torah and avodah, and He will certainly accept him when he returns to serve Him.

**E.** The above, that the time for *cheshbon hanefesh* is in the month of Elul, whereas throughout the year one must occupy himself with Torah and avodah *without calculations*, also fits with what was discussed at the previous farbrengen [22] regarding engaging in Torah study even in an undesirable standing and situation (even to the point of doubts in faith, G-d forbid), because the light within it returns him to the good [23].

One must understand: Is this statement of our Sages ("the light within it returns him to the good") in contradiction to the statement of our Sages [24] (which was also brought in the maamar of 12 Tammuz [25]) that "if he does not merit, it becomes a potion of death for him"?

The explanation for this is according to the words of the Alter Rebbe in *Hilchos Talmud Torah* [which, judging by the content and style, is a "middle ground" between the Shulchan Aruch, which is according to the revealed, and the Siddur, which is according to Kabbalah], and these are his words [26]:

"The Sages did not say that a person should always occupy himself with Torah and mitzvos even if not for their own sake, except when he fulfills the mitzvos that he learns in the Torah, only that he does not learn and fulfill for the sake of Heaven, but rather from fear of punishment... or from love of reward to receive a prize... But if he does not fulfill what he learns, he is called wicked, and about him the verse says [12], 'But to the wicked, G-d says, "What right have you to recite My statutes?" etc.'... And about him the Sages said, 'If he does not merit, his Torah becomes a potion of death for him.'"

#### 178

And afterwards he adds another opinion: "And some say [27] that nevertheless, a person should always occupy himself with Torah, because from 'not for its own sake' he can come to 'for its own sake,' to learn in order to observe and perform, for the light within it returns him to the good, as the Sages said regarding [28] 'They have forsaken Me and have not kept My Torah; if only they had forsaken Me but kept My Torah, the light within it would have returned them to the good.'"

And he concludes: "And so the Kabbalists said that all the Torah and mitzvos that a person does while he is still wicked, even though he adds strength to the *kelipos* for the moment, nevertheless, when he later returns in teshuvah in this incarnation or another incarnation, as it is written [29], 'that no one cast out be banished from Him,' then he extracts from the *kelipah* all the Torah and mitzvos, and they return to holiness upon his return. Therefore, he should never refrain from occupying himself."

Meaning: Even when his Torah becomes a potion of death for him, and he adds strength to the *kelipos* for the moment, nevertheless, he must occupy himself with Torah, and the light within it returns him to the good.

**F.** It should be added that the "light" that returns him to the good refers to the *pnimiyus* (inner dimension) of Torah, as discussed above [22], that the revealed dimension of Torah is the *ohr* (light) of Torah, and the *pnimiyus* of Torah is the *maor* (the luminary/source of light) of Torah.

And for us—this is connected with the *Nasi* of the *maor* (inner dimension) of Torah, who is the *Rebbe*.

From this it is understood that at every time and in every standing and situation, one must study the Chassidus of the Rebbe, and one does not need to precede it every time with calculations regarding his standing and situation, whether he is "at that level" ("*tzi er halt derbei*"). For even when he is in the lowest state, the light within it returns him to the good, that he will eventually do teshuvah (in this incarnation or another incarnation), for "no one cast out be banished from Him." And then, not only will he be purified from sins, iniquities, and transgressions, but furthermore, even the Torah that he brought down below through his study at that time [30] is elevated and returns to holiness.

**G.** Based on this, one can also explain the words of the Rebbe (Rayatz) [31] who described in a long story the difference between Chassidim themselves (in addition to the difference between Chassidim

#### 179

and those who are not Chassidim), between the Chassidim of Poland and the Chassidim of Chabad—that the Polish Chassidim have both this world and the World to Come, whereas the Chassidim of Chabad are "torn apart" both in this world and in the World to Come:

When a Chassid of the Polish Chassidim travels to his Rebbe—then the "Torah" is brief, and *shirayim* (especially on Yom Tov) are in abundance, at all the meals, and afterwards they go out in a dance, and thus the whole time passes in avodah with joy. And before he returns home, he enters the Rebbe to take leave of him, and asks for his blessing for everything he needs in children, life, and ample sustenance, for a grown daughter, for livelihood, etc., and the Rebbe promises him. And when he returns home and tells his wife about the requests and blessings they received from the Rebbe, she too is happy about his trip to the Rebbe. And when he wants to travel to his Rebbe again, she assists him by giving him provisions for the way, "little cakes with herring," and a few coins for the trip, etc.—so he has this world.

And after 120 years, when he comes to the World to Come, and the Heavenly Court asks about his standing and situation ("*vos hert zich mit im*"), *un es vert bitter*... he has a ready answer: I was a Chassid of my Rebbe, and "the righteous shall live by his faith" (*tzaddik be'emunaso yichyeh*), read not *yichyeh* (shall live) but *yechayeh* (shall give life) [32]. And when they ask him if he fulfilled everything the Rebbe commanded him, he replies: My Rebbe gave me blessings, and told me that I would be a keeper of Torah and mitzvos, that I would have a *shiur* in Torah study, etc., and this I did. And when they continue to ask him about "Know the G-d of your father" [33], "Have you gazed at the Chariot" [34], he replies: They taught me that one needs faith, as stated, "the righteous shall live by his faith," read not *yichyeh* but *yechayeh*, and faith I have. And they also taught me that one does not expound upon the *Maaseh Merkavah* and *Maaseh Bereishis* [35], and indeed I fulfilled this. And everywhere I saw the word "Atzilus," and certainly "Tzimtzum" or "Kav," I fled from it as far as a bowshot... for who am I and what am I, and "do not inquire into what is hidden from you" [36]!

And consequently, since he has faith above his head (a true *makif*), for all of Israel are "believers, the children of believers" [37], and especially the community of Chassidim ("*bei zei is doch nit sheyach tzu zogen*"), and he fulfilled the words of the Rebbe both in positive commandments and negative commandments—therefore, all of Gan Eden and the World to Come is not enough for him!...

#### 180

Whereas a Chassid of Chabad who travels to the Rebbe—he is pushed (not to receive "shirayim," for who even mentions that, but) to hear the *maamar*. And sometimes, because of the pushing, it is hard for him to hear and understand the *maamar*...

—A certain Yid, "an *olam'sher*," told me what remained in his memory from the Shabbos he was in Lubavitch: They pushed him so much during the recitation of the *maamar* that he only knew the question on the verse at the beginning of the *maamar*. The content of the *maamar* he did not understand, and towards the answer at the end of the *maamar* "he was already completely crushed" [*iz er shoin geven ingantzun tzukvetcht*] [apparently they knew who he was, and therefore, they pushed him in a manner of *hiddur mitzvah*...]. To taste something ("*iberchapn di hartz*") after the *maamar* was not possible, because there was a "chazarah," and afterwards the prayer, and after the *maamar*, the *chazarah*, and the prayer at length (at least a little), the time for Mincha prayer had already arrived. And thus the whole Shabbos day passed for him "a tired one, a crushed one, and a hungry one," and he didn't even know the *maamar*...—

And afterwards when he enters the Rebbe—he is *ashamed* to speak about his physical needs, livelihood, a grown daughter, etc. For after he has just heard a *maamar* from the Rebbe about Atzilus and above Atzilus, how can he take the Rebbe's time to tell him that his wife gives him no rest because she has no meat for Shabbos and black bread for the weekdays, the grocer no longer wants to give on credit, the creditors are making their lives miserable, etc... From the Rebbe he asks about love of Hashem and fear of Hashem, acceptance of the yoke, that he should be able to pray without foreign thoughts, that he should have the feeling of "in a word, Chassidus," and the like, and the Rebbe gives him his blessing for these requests. And when he returns home, his wife asks—knowing that the Rebbe's blessing is effective—what the Rebbe promised regarding livelihood, regarding the "dowry" for the grown daughter, etc. And he replies that about these matters he did not speak with the Rebbe at all, but rather about love and fear, the avodah of prayer without distractions from the troubles of livelihood... It is understood that he receives a "verdict" from her on the spot. And when he wants to travel to the Rebbe the following year, his wife shouts that it would be better if he hired himself out as a "chazzan" or "shammash" and earned a few rubles, and she does not let him travel, and does not give him anything, etc., etc.—so he has no this world.

And after 120 years, when he comes to the World to Come—they ask him what kind of Chassid you are, and he answers "with a breadth": "A Lubavitcher Chassid, a Chabadnik." If so—they continue to ask him—do you know what the acronym of Chabad is? And he replies: Certainly, *Chochma, Binah, Da'as*. And what is the meaning of this—that faith alone, which is in the category of *makif*, is not enough, but there must also be knowledge and comprehension, "Know the G-d of

#### 181

your father," specifically in the *pnimiyus*. If so—they tell him—let us examine your knowledge, "*vert doch bitter*"... and he is silent. They ask him: Perhaps the Rebbe did not teach you, did not demand of you? And he replies: The Rebbe taught me and demanded of me, I heard one *maamar*, and another *maamar*, and another *maamar*, and even when I did not have for the travel expenses, and I could not walk on foot, the Rebbe sent me the *maamar* in writing. And consequently, the investigation and interrogation begins: How is it possible, etc., and it is understood what the end of the matter is.

**H.** And here the question is asked: It is true that the "pain" is worthwhile for the sake of "knowledge" (as it is written [38], "He who increases knowledge increases pain"), but what is the benefit of the "pain" when there is no "knowledge"? And in our case, since in any event one does not learn Chassidus as required, "what is the worth of the whole affair"?!

The answer to this is that one must strive and do according to his ability, and then they assist him from above that he will eventually arrive at the truth of the matter.

This is similar to what is explained in *Torah Or* [39] regarding the obligation of Torah study day and night, that we find two extremes in this: "Even if a person only learned one chapter in the morning and one chapter in the evening, he has fulfilled the mitzvah of 'It shall not depart, etc.'" [40] [41]. And furthermore, as Rabbi Shimon bar Yochai says, "Even if a person only read the Shema in the morning and evening, he has fulfilled 'It shall not depart'" [41]. And on the other hand, Rabbi Shimon bar Yochai himself says [42], "Is it possible for a person to plow during plowing time, etc., what will become of the Torah?" Rather, one must learn Torah day and night, "This book of the Torah shall not depart from your mouth" ("words as they are written") [43]. Meaning, that anyone who is able is obligated to occupy himself with Torah day and night, and if he is able to occupy himself with Torah and does not, about him the Sages expounded [44], "For he has despised the word of Hashem, etc., he shall surely be cut off, etc." [45], in this world and the next. And anyone who is unable to occupy himself with Torah day and night (because from above he was not given the possibility for this for a reason that does not depend on his choice), since he must engage in business for the livelihood of his household, he fulfills his obligation with one chapter in the morning and one chapter in the evening—for "since it is impossible for him and he occupies himself as much as possible, Hashem will complete for him, etc., what he needs to attain" if he were to occupy himself with Torah day and night.

#### 182

And similarly in our case: It is true that now some of the Chassidim have not yet arrived at the fulfillment of the command "Know the G-d of your father" as required, they do not grasp the *Yichuda Ila'ah*, and sometimes not even the *Yichuda Tata'ah*. Nevertheless, since they exert themselves according to their ability, Hashem will certainly complete from above as required in perfection [46].

And this is also the general concept of "the light within it returns him to the good"—that in every standing and situation one must study the Chassidus of the Rebbe, the light in the Torah, and through this he will eventually arrive at the desired perfection.

**I.** However, the above, that one must occupy himself with Torah and avodah without calculations regarding his standing and situation because in the end the light within it returns him to the good—this is only regarding the *Nefesh HaElokis*, which certainly "no one cast out be banished from Him," and he will eventually do teshuvah, and his Torah also returns with him. In addition to this, there must also be the refinement of the *Nefesh HaBehamis* to transform it into holiness.

And for this comes the instruction in the *parsha* of the week that we read on Shabbos Mevarchim the month of Elul, the time for *cheshbon hanefesh*, regarding the signs of purity of animals, "This is the animal that you may eat, etc.," which hints at the refinement (purity) of the *Nefesh HaBehamis*.

By way of introduction—that two types of animals, pure and impure, were stated in the Torah, for even in conduct according to the Torah there can be an impure animal, as the Ramban [47] says that there can be a "scoundrel with the permission of the Torah" (meaning, that it seems to him that this is with the permission of the Torah, because in truth he is violating the command "You shall be holy," a primary and root command). And therefore, there is a need for refinement and discernment to know if this is a pure animal or an impure one [48]. And for this, the Torah gave two signs: (a) splits the hoof, (b) chews the cud—signs that are connected to and even effect the purity.

**J.** And the concept of this:

Even in an animal there is a primary difference between the head and the foot (as is understood also from the laws of *treifos*, etc.), that the foot specifically is close to the earth. The parallel in the *Nefesh HaBehamis* is that only the power of action (foot) is placed in physical, earthly matters, as is necessary, but the higher powers (head) even of the *Nefesh HaBehamis* do not need to be placed in earthly matters.

#### 183

—The Rebbe (Rashab) once said to one of the Chassidim, a talented man who involved himself in the business of galoshes: Feet in galoshes I have seen, but a head in galoshes?!...—

And furthermore: Even in the foot that is close to the earth there must be a hoof—a *parsa* (with an *aleph*), whose concept is separation and concealment—that even the power of action (foot) must be in separation from earthly matters. And on the other hand, the hoof must be split and completely separated ("*durch un durch*")—that through this the light of Hashem will shine below, even in earthly matters, that he will see Divinity in them [49]. In the words of the Alter Rebbe in the daily *Tanya* lesson [50]: "That even in earthly matters they should not be separated from the One of Truth."

And in addition to this, there must also be the sign of chewing the cud—that regarding every physical thing he does, he must first refine it and return and refine it again, "chew it over well," whether to do it and how to do it, etc.

And when these two signs exist, then one can make the animal into a *pure* animal—meaning, that the physicality that is necessary due to the body (the animal within him) will be in a manner of purity [51].

**K.** And an additional lesson—from the signs of purity in a bird:

In a bird—one does not rely on signs alone, and one should not eat any bird except with a tradition received about it that it is pure. "And the hunter is trusted to say, 'This bird was permitted to me by the master hunter... for he is an expert in the impure species stated in the Torah and their names'" [52].

The lesson from this in a person's avodah—that one should not rely on his own intellect. For a person can conduct himself according to the Shulchan Aruch, and even conduct himself according to his intellect *lifnim mishuras hadin* (beyond the letter of the law), and be found at the time of action in the lowest depths, G-d forbid. There is a need for a tradition, "tradition" (*masores*) also from the language of *mesirah* (handing over) and *hiskashrus* (connection), a connection to the Rebbe, and a "master hunter," a Rebbe who occupies himself with saving the souls of Israel from the tactic of the *yetzer hara*, and is an expert in them and their names.

**L.** And one should also connect this with the beginning of the *parsha*—"See, I am setting before you today a blessing and a curse":

By way of introduction—that this verse was said by Moshe, and includes also the "extension of Moshe" in every generation [53], the *Nasi* of the generation, who is called "Anochi," like the "Anochi" from above, "I am who I am" [54], the level of *Keter* [55]. And the parallel in the totality of the souls of Israel is that "Anochi" refers to the level of *Yechidah*, the level of the *Nasi* of the generation.

And about this it is said, "See, I am setting before you today a blessing and a curse"—for it is possible that only when one receives a *blessing* from the Rebbe and the blessing is fulfilled, then he sees that the Rebbe is a Rebbe and connects to him, whereas in an opposite situation, G-d forbid, then doubts fall upon him, etc. Therefore, the verse clarifies, "See, I am setting before you today a blessing and a curse," meaning, that even the opposite situation, G-d forbid, is in a manner that "I am setting."

The explanation for this—that seemingly how is it possible that from the level of "Anochi" the opposite of a blessing should be drawn down—one can say that the intention is so that "he should get through with it" [56], or that the intention is only that he should accept upon himself [that he will come to the Rebbe—by going to the Ohel, giving a *pidyon*, or in another way—and say (to Hashem through the Rebbe) that he accepts upon himself] and through this he will be exempt from it [57] [similar to Rashi on the verse [58], "The blessing which you will hear," "on condition that you hear," meaning, that through your hearing (that he hears and accepts upon himself) then "I am setting before you a blessing"], or that the truth is that no evil descends from above [59] and everything is good, only that it is not grasped due to its greatness and abundance of goodness [60]. As explained in *Likutei Torah* [61] that even the curses are only blessings, "they are all blessings" [62], and furthermore, blessings of the highest level that cannot be revealed except in the language of the opposite of a blessing (so that the evil eye should not have power over them, or the accusation of the attribute of judgment).

#### 185

And through the power of the connection to the Rebbe—that he will recognize that all matters are drawn from above through the Rebbe, and everything is good—then the curses will be transformed into blessings in practice and in revelation, and he will see tangibly that "it is found in all worlds (and even) more than in his lifetime" [63].

And until the true perfection in all this—when the complete Redemption will be soon, and the Rebbe will lead us towards our righteous Moshiach, amen.

#### 186

The significance of the eighteenth of Elul has been explained in many places[1](#_ftn916) in the sichos of the Rebbe Rayatz: it is the birthday of the Baal Shem Tov (in the year 5458), the day his holy teacher and mentor, Achiyah HaShiloni, revealed himself to him (in the year 5484), the day of the Baal Shem Tov’s own revelation (in the year 5494)[2](#_ftn917), the birthday of the Alter Rebbe (in the year 5505), and the day that marks the "beginning of the avodah for the coming new year."[3](#_ftn918)

Regarding the third day of Selichos, the Rebbe Rayatz related[4](#_ftn919) that this day "is the day designated and set aside for the glory of the Alter Rebbe for all generations, to recite [a maamar][5](#_ftn920) only before his chosen son, the Mitteler Rebbe. When my grandfather, the Tzemach Tzedek, was about ten years old, he also recited it before him—a maamar of Chassidus in private—and instructed him that no one should know of it. My grandfather, the Tzemach Tzedek, would recite a maamar of the Alter Rebbe on the third day of Selichos before my grandfather, the Rebbe Maharash, and instructed him that no one should know of it. My grandfather, the Rebbe Maharash, would recite a drush on the third day of Selichos before my father, the Rebbe Rashab. And on the third day of Selichos, 5655, my father, the Rebbe Rashab, saw fit to recite this drush before me,[6](#_ftn921) and instructed me to keep it secret until the time he would command me to reveal it."

***

Regarding the avodah of the days from Chai Elul onwards, the Rebbe Rayatz explained[7](#_ftn922) "that the month of Elul is the time for the revelations of 'I am to my beloved, and my beloved is to me.'"

#### 187

The preparations for these revelations correspond to the essence and nature of the revelation itself... [This includes] teshuvah and a broken heart during the twelve days from Chai Elul until Rosh Hashanah. The first night of Rosh Hashanah is the actual revelation of "I am to my beloved," together with the kabbalas ol of mesiras nefesh following the preparation. During these twelve days, there must be a cheshbon hanefesh for the twelve months of the year—one day for each month. Chai Elul is the day for the cheshbon hanefesh of the past month of Tishrei. Erev Rosh Hashanah is the day set aside for the cheshbon hanefesh of the month of Elul, which is the final preparation for the avodah of Rosh Hashanah, to receive a kesivah v'chasimah tovah in the study of Torah, the performance of mitzvos, and the avodah shebalev, with a good and sweet year in material terms."

***

For many years, the significance of Chai Elul and the third day of Selichos remained hidden, but now permission has been granted to reveal them, and the Rebbe Rayatz publicized them to the ends of the earth. And although one might ask, "Have the generations improved?"—the opposite is true. Precisely because the concealments and darkness of *ikvesa d'meshicha* have intensified so greatly, "the treasures of precious wealth that were hidden and sealed from the sight of all" until now are being opened.

Especially now, after his histalkus, it is incumbent upon each and every one of us to strengthen ourselves and connect to the *ilana d'chayei* and our Nasi, the Rebbe Rayatz, with greater intensity and strength. And with the help of his treasures and the treasures of his ancestors—which he opened and entrusted to us—we must remove the concealments and darkness, which is also a preparation and qualification to receive a kesivah v'chasimah tovah.

**Menachem Schneerson**

*Erev Shabbos Kodesh, 13 Elul, 5710,*
*Brooklyn, N.Y.*

## General Letter

#### 188

B”H, 18th of Elul, 5711
Brooklyn, N.Y.

To our brothers and sisters, the sons and daughters of Israel,
wherever they may be,
May Hashem grant them life,

Peace and Blessing!

As we stand on the threshold of the New Year, which is approaching for us and for all the sons and daughters of Israel for good and for blessing, each and every one of us awakens to take stock of everything that occurred during the passing year—what we have done and what has been done with us. We resolve to improve our ways and turn in tefillah to our Father in Heaven, that He may inscribe and seal us—each man, each woman, and all that is theirs—for a good and sweet year, both materially and spiritually[1].

Our Sages of blessed memory have said that through the giving of tzedakah[2] to the poor, tefillah is effective in drawing down a good life, geulah and salvation, parnasah and sustenance.

Now, a person[3]—like all created beings, and even the angels on high[4]—possesses both a body and a soul. Just as there is a poor person in terms of the body and its needs, so too is there a poor person in terms of the soul and its needs. Therefore, regarding tzedakah as well, there is tzedakah in the material sense and tzedakah in the spiritual sense. As our Sages said (Tanna D’bei Eliyahu Rabbah, ch. 27): “'When you see the naked, you shall cover him'—how? If you see a person who possesses no words of Torah, bring him into your home, teach him Krias Shema and tefillah, teach him [other matters], and encourage him in the performance of mitzvos.”

Just as the request of each and every one of us is to be inscribed and sealed for good

#### 189

materially and spiritually, so too must each and every one of us strive in both material tzedakah and spiritual tzedakah alike, especially during the days of Elul and Tishrei.

And as we come in these days before the King of kings, the Holy One, Blessed be He, may each and every one of us—all of us—have a great account[5], great according to one’s own capacity, of the tzedakah one has performed to assist the poor in material matters, and the tzedakah one has performed to assist the poor in spiritual matters.

Just as with material tzedakah[6], even one who is poor materially is obligated to give—for there is no poor person who cannot find a way to help a fellow poor person—so too with spiritual tzedakah, even one who is poor spiritually is obligated to give. For there is no man or woman in Israel who cannot influence other sons and daughters of Israel to bring them closer to yiras shamayim, Torah, and mitzvos.

And *l’fum gamla shichna*[7]—according to the camel is the load—those who are wealthy materially, and those who are wealthy spiritually—the Torah scholars, the *yoshvei ohel*, and the students of the yeshivos—must expend with an open hand from their wealth and their knowledge for the rescue, healing, and restoration of the souls and bodies of their brothers and sisters, may they live.

May our Father, the Father of Mercy[8], inscribe and seal each and every one of us among all of Israel for a good and sweet year, in goodness that is visible and revealed, materially and spiritually, and may He bring us, speedily in our days, the true and complete geulah through Moshiach Tzidkeinu, Amen.

Blessing and being blessed with a good inscription and seal,

**Menachem Mendel ben Chana Schneerson**

Son-in-law of the Nasi, our holy master,
our teacher and Rebbe (of Lubavitch), may the merit of the righteous protect us.

## 18th of Elul, 5710

**190**

*Unedited*

**A.** Regarding the 18th of Elul, the Rebbe Rayatz writes that it is the birthday of the Baal Shem Tov and the Alter Rebbe. Based on the well-known principle that on a person's birthday his *mazal* is strengthened (the source for this—like every matter in the *nistar* that is also hinted at in the *nigleh*—is in the *Yerushalmi*), it is understood that on the 18th of Elul, the powers of the Baal Shem Tov and the Alter Rebbe are strengthened.
