Regarding the mission of the Baal Shem Tov and the Alter Rebbe, the Rebbe Rayatz explains that the Baal Shem Tov paved a path for every single Jew, even the simplest of the simple, to be able to serve Hashem, and the Alter Rebbe showed how every single Jew, even the simplest of the simple, can serve Hashem. In another place, it is brought that the Baal Shem Tov erected a ladder that everyone (even simple people) could climb, and the Alter Rebbe showed how to ascend that ladder.

Although the path or the ladder is not explicitly defined there, it is understood from what the Rebbe Rayatz wrote: that the essence of the Baal Shem Tov is *ahavas Hashem*, *ahavas haTorah*, and *ahavas Yisrael*, or (as it is sometimes brought) in reverse order: *ahavas Yisrael*, *ahavas haTorah*, and *ahavas Hashem* (from above to below or from below to above).

**B.** These three loves—*ahavas Yisrael*, *ahavas haTorah*, and *ahavas Hashem*—are connected to one another, to the point that they are all one.

In the language of the *Mishnah*: "Whoever the spirit of his fellow creatures finds pleasure in, the spirit of the Omnipresent finds pleasure in; and whoever the spirit of his fellow creatures does not find pleasure in, the spirit of the Omnipresent does not find pleasure in."

—It is worth noting the precision of the word *briyos* (creatures) specifically, similar to what the Alter Rebbe writes

**191**

in *Tanya*, Chapter 32, in explaining the Sages' statement, "Love the *briyos* and draw them close to Torah," that "even those who are distant from the Torah of Hashem and His service, and are therefore called merely *briyos* (creatures)," meaning that they have no advantage or merit other than being *briyos* of the *Ribono shel Olam* [similar to the story in the *Gemara* where Rabbi Elazar encountered a man who was extremely ugly and said, "How ugly is that man," and the man replied, "Go and tell the *Craftsman who made me*..."], "one must pull them with cords of love... to draw them close to Torah and the service of Hashem." The same applies here: even regarding those who are "merely *briyos*," they said, "Whoever the spirit of his fellow creatures finds pleasure in, the spirit of the Omnipresent finds pleasure in..."

From this, it is understood that when there is a lack of *ahavas Yisrael* ("the spirit of his fellow creatures does not find pleasure in him"), it is proof that there is a lack of *ahavas haTorah* and *ahavas Hashem* ("the spirit of the Omnipresent does not find pleasure in him").

More specifically:

*Ahavas Hashem* alone can lead to isolation in general, and specifically from simple people. Similarly, *ahavas haTorah* alone can cause one to become self-important and, consequently, isolate himself from the rest of *Bnei Yisrael*. Therefore, we emphasize that one should not be satisfied with *ahavas Hashem* and *ahavas haTorah*; there must also be *ahavas Yisrael*. Furthermore, a deficiency in *ahavas Yisrael* indicates a deficiency in *ahavas Hashem* and *ahavas haTorah* as well.

*Ahavas Yisrael* provides strength in *ahavas haTorah* and ensures that *ahavas Hashem* is genuine. *Ahavas Hashem* and *ahavas haTorah* act and lead to *ahavas Yisrael*.

**C.** In this matter, there is a special empowerment on the 18th of Elul:

Even if throughout the entire year there was a deficiency in these three loves and their connection to one another, when the 18th of Elul arrives—the birthday of the Baal Shem Tov and the Alter Rebbe, on which their powers are strengthened—additional strength is granted in these three loves and their interconnection.

Simply put, this means that the recognition in the mind and the feeling in the heart are achieved that it is impossible to divide or separate the three loves—*ahavas Yisrael*, *ahavas haTorah*, and *ahavas Hashem*—since they are all one, just as *Yisrael*, *Orayta* (Torah), and *Kudsha Brich Hu* (Hashem) are all one.

\*

**192**

**D.** Regarding the significance of the twelve days from the 18th of Elul until *Rosh Hashanah*, the Rebbe Rayatz explains that during these days one must perform a *cheshbon hanefesh* for the twelve months of the year, one day for each month, starting with the 18th of Elul itself, which is the day for the *cheshbon* of the past month of *Tishrei*, during which many of those present here were together with the Rebbe Rayatz while he was alive in this world.

And since the Rebbe Rayatz says that the 18th of Elul is the day of the *cheshbon* for the past month of *Tishrei*, surely *he himself* is also performing a *cheshbon* for the past month of *Tishrei*.

By way of introduction: there are those who fear that after the *histalkus*, one becomes "free among the dead," but the truth is that it is not said of everyone that they are "free among the dead." As explained in *Sefer Chassidim*, *tzaddikim* who are called "living" even in their death are not like other dead people (like any other person) who are exempt from *mitzvos*. Rather, even after the *histalkus*, they have all the obligations as they did before.

Based on this, one can explain what is said in the *Gemara* that after the *histalkus* of *Rabbeinu HaKadosh*, he would come to his home every *Erev Shabbos* for *Kiddush HaYom*, and *Sefer Chassidim* adds that he would "exempt the public (the members of the household) from their obligation of *Kiddush HaYom*" (even though one who is not obligated in *Kiddush HaYom* cannot exempt those who are obligated)—because *Rabbeinu HaKadosh* is not included among other dead people who are free from *mitzvos*; rather, he remains under the obligation of *Kiddush HaYom* (and all other obligations) as before.

Based on this, the story brought in *Emek HaMelech*—that they included Avraham Avinu as the tenth for a *minyan*—is also understood, because he is not included among other dead people who are free from *mitzvos*.

The explanation for this is that for general souls, whose entire existence throughout their lives was dedicated to sacrificing themselves entirely for their students, those connected to them, and all of

**193**

*Klal Yisrael*, there is no side reason that could separate them from their primary mission. Thus, even now, they do not separate from their flock—who are also currently in this world and are obligated in all matters of Torah and *mitzvos*—to help and assist them in all their affairs, as mentioned, to exempt the public. The concept of being "free" does not apply to them; rather, they are obligated in all matters as before, and on the contrary, with even greater intensity, since the limitations of the body have been removed (as it is in *Tanya* that even a *tzaddik gamur* who serves Hashem with fear and love and *taanug* is "someone who loves," which is not the case after the limitations of the body are removed).

From this, it is also understood regarding the *cheshbon* of the 18th of Elul for the past month of *Tishrei*—that even after the *histalkus*, he is obligated in this and performs it as before, just as in the previous year, two and three years ago, and thirty years ago, ever since he dedicated himself to his students and those connected to him to help and assist them in all matters.

**E.** The *cheshbon* that the Rebbe Rayatz performs on the 18th of Elul for the past month of *Tishrei* also concerns all the blessings that were given by him during the past month of *Tishrei*.

The Rebbe Rayatz told me that he never remained in bankruptcy, *chas v'shalom*, and was faithful and confident in the payment of all his obligations. Since this is so, he will certainly fulfill all his blessings.

The Rebbe, for his part—since he promised, it is certainly in his power to fulfill, for otherwise he would not have promised. However, there is a condition to the matter: the *hiskashrus* to the Rebbe, and the belief that even now he can fulfill and will certainly fulfill his blessings.

The Rebbe Rayatz once spoke about *machshavos zaros* (alien thoughts) during *tefillah*, that it is like a person knocking on the King's door, and when the King comes and opens the door for him, he leaves and goes on his way. The analogy is as the Sages said, "A person should always arrange the praise of the Omnipresent and then pray," for the preparation of arranging the praise of the Omnipresent is like knocking on the King's door, and the *tefillah* itself is the *eis ratzon* (time of favor) when the King opens the door. When one thinks *machshavos zaros* during *tefillah*, it is as if he leaves and goes on his way at the very moment the King is opening the door for him.

Regarding our matter: after we all immersed in the *mikveh* today, and gathered

**194**

for a *farbrengen*, in accordance with the Rebbe's will—and furthermore, the *farbrengen* itself is within the four walls of the Rebbe—it is as if we have knocked on the Rebbe's door. Consequently, it is not appropriate to get up and leave... rather, on the contrary, one should utilize this opportunity to connect to the Rebbe, by studying his Torah and visualizing his face.

All those who saw the Rebbe should visualize his face, *kal v'chomer* from what is said in the *Gemara* that *Rabbeinu HaKadosh* said, "The reason I am sharper than my colleagues is that I saw Rabbi Meir *from behind*, and had I seen him from the front, etc.," and we even find in the *Yerushalmi* that "Rabbi Meir's *staff* was in the hand (of one of the elders of the Galilee) and it would teach him wisdom."

And through the *hiskashrus* to the Rebbe, all his blessings will certainly be fulfilled.

\*

**F.** It is known that in "Elul," the name of the month of *cheshbon* for the service of the entire year, the initials of all the matters of service—the three lines of Torah, *avodah*, and *gemilus chasadim*—are hinted at:

"Elul" is an acronym for "*Ani l'dodi v'dodi li*"—which is the matter of *tefillah*, the line of *avodah*, as the Sages said, "What is the *avodah* of the heart? This is *tefillah*."

"Elul" is an acronym for "*Ish l're'ehu u'matanos la'evyonim*"—which is the matter of *tzedakah* and *gemilus chasadim*.

And "Elul" is an acronym for "*Aneh l'yado v'samti lach*" which is said regarding the cities of refuge, which hints at the line of Torah, as follows.

**G.** One must first explain the general concept of the cities of refuge to which anyone who kills a person unintentionally shall flee—"And if he did not lie in wait, but Hashem brought it to his hand, then I will appoint for you a place to which he may flee"—by way of introduction:

Among the nations of the world, there is a punishment of sitting in prison. Not so in Torah, *l'havdil*, of which it is said, "For it is your wisdom and your understanding in the eyes of the nations." We do not

**195**

find a punishment of sitting in prison. We do find that "they place him in a *kipah* (dome)," but that is not a punishment of sitting in prison; rather, it is a preparation and beginning of another punishment (the death penalty).

The reason for this:

The nations of the world know about (and have) only one world, this world of ours, in which they live seventy or eighty years, as it is written, "The days of our years are seventy years, or if by strength, eighty years," and at most, "His days shall be one hundred and twenty years." Consequently, when a person sins and blemishes and strays from the path, and causes *damage and ruin to the world* (the only world that exists), they catch him and lock him alone in a prison so that he will not cause damage and ruin to the world.

And even though by doing so they take from him the freedom necessary to fulfill his role, and consequently, the entire concept of reward and punishment and the entire purpose of his creation for the rest of his life is nullified, whereby he becomes an "ex-person"—this is a necessary thing, without choice, for the sake of preventing damage and ruin to the many, for when it comes to an individual and the many, the many outweigh the individual, and it is necessary to sacrifice the individual for the sake of the many.

But according to *Torah*, *l'havdil*, which is a Torah of life and a Torah of kindness, "good to Heaven and good to the creatures"—it is impossible to sacrifice one thing for the sake of another, nor to sacrifice the individual and cause him damage for the sake of preventing damage to the many.

[And in the style of the *nigleh* of Torah—for everything has a source in the *nigleh* of Torah—"everything that is fixed is like half and half, and is not nullified in the majority."]

Torah does not cause damage, and even the punishments of the Torah are not for the sake of punishment alone, but for the sake of *rectifying* the sin. Therefore, there is no such thing as a punishment of sitting in prison, for by doing so, one denies him the possibility and ability to fulfill his role and mission.

"Days were formed, and for Him one of them." For every person, a limited time of days is set in the Heavenly Court to fulfill his role and mission, and every single moment of his life

**196**

must be utilized for the fulfillment of his role and mission. Therefore, it is impossible to punish him by sitting in prison, for by doing so, one prevents him from fulfilling his role and mission in life.

In every situation and state, even if he has sinned and blemished and strayed from the path, the obligation remains upon him to continue in the fulfillment of his role and mission in life. "What business do you have with the secrets of the Merciful One?" As the Rebbe (Rashab) writes, that we do not know anything in the secret of the *birurim*. Therefore, regardless of his status and situation, he must fulfill every *mitzvah* that comes his way, without calculating how he will fulfill a law in *Choshen Mishpat* while he is still lacking in the fulfillment of the laws of *Orach Chaim*, and the like.

[And the death penalty is different—(a) that it is not in a way that we sacrifice his existence for the benefit of the many, but rather, *from his own perspective* (as an individual) he deserves the death penalty, for his own *rectification*; (b) and it is not in a way that we deny him the possibility to fulfill his role and mission in life, because after the Torah (and consequently the Heavenly Court) rules that he does not need to live anymore, *he no longer has* a role and mission.]

From this, it is understood that the content of sitting in a city of refuge for one who kills a person unintentionally is not a punishment of sitting *in prison*—because even when he is in a situation where the "avenger of blood" can kill him, the obligation remains upon him to fulfill his role and mission, and consequently, it is impossible to put him in prison and prevent him from fulfilling his role and mission—but rather a punishment of *exile*, as the *Chinuch* writes, "that he should suffer the pain of exile... that a person is separated from his loved ones and his homeland, etc." But, together with this, they must give him everything necessary for the fulfillment of his role and mission as a free person, to the extent that "a student who is exiled, his teacher is exiled with him," meaning that even though he is a student who is not proper, nevertheless, his teacher is exiled with him, so that he can study Torah from his teacher as before.

**H.** The explanation of the concept of the city of refuge to which one who kills a person unintentionally shall flee—in the service of man:

"Kills a person" refers to one who sins and blemishes and strays from the path, whereby he blemishes his soul, the *nefesh elokis* and the *nefesh habahamis*. However, the *nefesh elokis* is in a state of

**197**

eternal existence, and it is impossible to separate it from *Elokus*, for even at the time of the sin it was in faith with Him, may He be blessed. But regarding the *nefesh habahamis*, this is in the category of "killing a person."

The reason he is called "kills a person *unintentionally*"—one can say, based on the Sages' statement, "A person does not commit a sin unless a spirit of folly enters him." And since, were it not for the "spirit of folly," he would not have committed a sin, it is impossible to judge and punish him for killing a person, for even among the nations of the world they do not punish a fool, *rachmana litzlan*.

[And based on this, one can explain what is said in the *Gemara* that "a Sanhedrin that executes once in seventy years is called destructive... Rabbi Tarfon and Rabbi Akiva say, had we been in the Sanhedrin, no person would ever have been executed"—for seemingly it is not understood: when according to Torah there is an obligation of death, it is impossible to waive and exempt him, and the Sages said, "Whoever says the Holy One, blessed be He, is lenient, his life will be waived"—because the *Tannaim* and *Amoraim* who studied Torah (together with the light in the Torah) as it is in its root above, the light of the Torah as it is a Torah of *kindness* shone for them, and consequently, they were able to find for each person a *limud zechus* (similar to the manner of service of the *tzaddik* Rabbi Levi Yitzchak of Berditchev). And it starts from the general *limud zechus*—"A person does not commit a sin unless a spirit of folly enters him."]

And nevertheless, they do not exempt him entirely, because he is guilty for having allowed ("*er hot zugelozzt*") the spirit of folly to enter and rule over him. Therefore, they judge him (at least) as one who kills a person *unintentionally*.

And the rectification for this is—"And he shall flee to one of these cities," cities of refuge:

It is in the *Gemara*, "From where do we know that words of Torah provide refuge? As it is written, 'Bezer in the wilderness, etc.' [and it is written after it] 'And this is the Torah,'" meaning that "city of refuge" refers to *Torah*.

The explanation for this is based on the Sages' statement, "The Holy One, blessed be He, said to man, 'My lamp is in your hand, and your lamp is in My hand. My lamp is in your hand—this is the Torah, as it is written, "For the *mitzvah* is a lamp and the Torah is light." And your lamp is in My hand—this is the soul, as it is written, "The lamp of Hashem is the soul of man." If you have guarded My lamp, I will guard your lamp.'"

**198**

From this, it is understood that the Torah is the *vitality of the soul*, and furthermore, that the Torah is also *the vitality of the *nefesh habahamis*, as is understood from the fact that for *Bnei Yisrael*, even the vitality of the physical body is from the *nefesh elokis* (as explained in *Tanya* and at length in *Kuntres Umaayan*). Therefore, through taking refuge in Torah (words of Torah provide refuge) from which the vitality of the soul is drawn, the rectification of "killing a person (unintentionally)" is achieved.

And this is the content of the hint in the initials of "Elul," "Aneh l'yado v'samti lach"—that when one performs a *cheshbon hanefesh* of the entire year and sees that he is lacking here and there, and there are undesirable matters that are in the category of killing a person unintentionally ("Aneh l'yado"), one should not fall into despair, *chas v'shalom*, because the Holy One, blessed be He, says, "And I will appoint for you a place to which he may flee," cities of refuge, which is the matter of Torah, "words of Torah provide refuge."

**I.** One must add and be precise in the teaching, "From where do we know that words of Torah provide refuge? As it is written, 'Bezer in the wilderness, etc.' and 'And this is the Torah'"—that they also copy from the verse the word "in the wilderness" (even though for the teaching itself it is enough to write "Bezer, etc., and this is the Torah," without the word "in the wilderness")—that the refuge of Torah is effective even when one is found "*in the wilderness*."

By way of introduction:

When one approaches an American Jew and tells him about the existence of "Torah," he asks: "Torah"—how many years has it existed? And they answer him: approximately 3,300 years—he reacts with wonder: If so, then this is ancient history!

"What is—he continues to ask—this Torah?" And they answer him: The Torah teaches a person how to behave. In the morning, when he rises from his sleep, he must wash his hands, and afterwards he must go to the synagogue together with the ten *batlanim* to pray in public.

"Prayer—he continues to ask—how much time does it take?" "Not much—they answer him—approximately thirty minutes." "Nu, and afterwards—he continues to ask—what do you do?" "Afterwards—they answer him—the Rebbe says that one must say a *shiur* of *Tehillim*." "And what afterwards—he continues to ask?" "Afterwards, they answer him, there must be fixed times for Torah study."

—"Terrible," he answers, "this is a waste of time..."

**199**

"Nu, *m'ila*, and what next," he continues to ask? "Afterwards, they answer him, one must eat breakfast, whereby one avoids several illnesses."

—"What kind of business is that," he asks in astonishment, "in these days they have discovered new things, and what are you telling me about the Torah that was written 3,300 years ago?!"...

"And in any case—he continues to ask—after all this, am I already free?" "Yes, they answer him, afterwards you can go to your business, but you must be careful of theft, trespassing, *lashon hara*. You must separate a *maaser* from the profits in the business. On *Shabbos* and *Yom Tov* the business must be closed, and even on weekdays, you must stop in the middle of business and return to the synagogue among the ten *batlanim* and pray *Mincha*, and at night you must pray *Maariv*, and after *Maariv* you can eat 'supper'."

"And after all the 'troubles' that one must go through during the entire day—he continues to ask—am I already completely free? Can I watch 'television', go to a 'movie', play 'bridge', and the like?" —"No, they answer him, the *Gemara* says that 'the night was not created... except for study,' meaning that at night one must study Torah."

"Gemara—he asks—what is it, and how long has it existed?" And they answer him, approximately 1,900 years ago there were Ravina and Rav Ashi who arranged the *Gemara*, and it is written there, as mentioned, that one must utilize the night to study Torah! "And afterwards one must read *Krias Shema* and do a 'cheshbon hanefesh' for the entire day!"

"If so—he concludes the conversation—this is not for me, because with such schedules it is impossible to do 'business'!"...

And seemingly he can argue: Since he is found alone in the "*wilderness*," a wilderness in spirituality—how can he be careful of "fiery serpents and scorpions," and not only that, but also influence others?! And the answer to this is that he has the power from Avraham Avinu, "Avraham was one," who is called "Avram the Hebrew," because "the entire world was on one side and he was on the other side."

And this is also the precision "Bezer *in the wilderness*, etc., and this is the Torah":

**200**

Even when one is found "*in the wilderness*," through the Torah ("and this is the Torah") there is the power to be in a state and situation of "*Bezer*," a fortified city surrounded by a wall, doors, and a bar, a wall through which protection is achieved against the undesirable influence of the wilderness, and doors and a bar that one opens from time to time for the sake of the influence of "Bezer" upon the "wilderness" (according to the instruction and supervision of the guardians of the wall, who determine when and for how long to open the doors, whom to take out from Bezer to the wilderness and whom to bring in from the wilderness to Bezer).

And thereby one achieves the purpose of "Bezer in the wilderness"—that not only does "Bezer" remain in its perfection, but furthermore, the influence of "Bezer" is achieved upon the "wilderness," that in return for the fact that the "wilderness" by itself is "a land... where no man dwells," it will finally be fulfilled as it is written, "He turns the wilderness into a pool of water... and He settles there the hungry, and they establish a city to dwell in, and they sow fields and plant vineyards, etc."

**J.** However, in taking refuge in Torah to save "one who kills a person unintentionally," there is a condition—"And this is the Torah which *Moshe* placed," that it must be "the Torah of Moshe" specifically:

In order to be saved from the "avenger of blood," "he is the *Satan*, he is the *Yetzer Hara*, he is the Angel of Death," the decision to behave according to the Torah is not enough, because the *Yetzer Hara* who is called "the clever one" can sway a person's intellect (through the desire that sways the intellect) so that it *seems to him* that he is behaving according to the Torah. And as the *Ramban* writes, a person can be a "scoundrel with the permission of the Torah."

And the advice for this is—"And this is the Torah which *Moshe* placed"—that the conduct according to the Torah should be according to the instruction of Moshe specifically, and the extension of Moshe in every generation, the Moshe of our generation.

One must behave according to the instructions of Moshe in all matters, and for example regarding the study of Torah ("And this is the Torah which Moshe placed")—that the study must be *with fear of Heaven*, and is achieved by preceding it with *tefillah*, as the Sages say, "From the synagogue to the *Beis Medrash*" (prayer and afterwards Torah), which comes after the preparation of "One should not stand to pray except with a heavy head."

And through the fulfillment of the instructions of Moshe ("the Torah of Moshe") one succeeds in passing through

**201**

the wilderness ("Bezer in the wilderness, etc., and this is the Torah which Moshe placed") and being saved from "fiery serpents and scorpions,"

—During the war, there was a group of *Anash* in a certain place who could not establish contact with the Rebbe to ask for his opinion regarding their travel, and they behaved according to the well-known rule that in such a case one asks an "ordinary person" and does the *opposite*... and thereby they succeeded in saving their lives, etc.—

And thus one continues in the fulfillment of the instructions of Moshe of our generation, and in the *hiskashrus* to him, until the coming of *Mashiach*, and even afterwards.

—There are those who dream dreams and think that with the coming of *Mashiach* there will be some other Rebbe ("*eppes an anderer Rebbe*"). The truth is that when *Mashiach* comes, we will have our Rebbe ("*mir velen hoben undzer Rebbe'n*"). It will be the same Rebbe and the same *Chassidim*.

The Rebbe told me that his father, the Rebbe, would place the *Afikoman* of the household members together with his own *Afikoman*, and when he asked him the reason for this, he replied that the *Afikoman* is a remembrance of the *Korban Pesach* which was eaten in a group by all the members of the household, and so it will be when *Mashiach* comes. From this it is understood that even when *Mashiach* comes, all those who belong to the Rebbe (the members of the household) will be together with the Rebbe.

**K.** One must add in the explanation of the continuation of the verse—"And this is the Torah which Moshe placed *before* the children of Israel":

"Before" is (also) an expression of *inwardness* (*pnimiyus*), and the meaning of "And this is the Torah which Moshe placed before the children of Israel" is that Moshe exerted himself ("*Moshe hot zich opgegeben*") to reach ("*derlangen*") *the inwardness of Bnei Yisrael* (and everyone knows how difficult it is to reach the inwardness).

From this it is understood that the *hiskashrus* to Moshe must be "as water reflects the face to the face"—to the inwardness of Moshe.

In the language of the verse: "To You, my heart said, 'Seek My face'; Your face, Hashem, I will seek," that through the revelation of the inwardness of the soul ("face") one reaches the inwardness of Above ("Your face, Hashem"), and the same applies to the Rebbe, the Moshe of the generation, who is the intermediary that connects.

**202**

Those who until now have "taken" the externality of the Rebbe must now exert themselves to "take" his inwardness, and those who in the past also took the "inwardness" must add and take the inwardness of the inwardness.

**L.** And in this matter there is a special empowerment on the 18th of Elul—the birthday of the Baal Shem Tov and the Alter Rebbe:

Our Rebbes, the *Nesi'im* of the generations, are the replacements of the Baal Shem Tov and the Alter Rebbe. Consequently, all those who are connected to the Rebbe [even those who connected to the Rebbe after their Rebbe passed away and they could not remain without a Rebbe], are connected to the Baal Shem Tov and the Alter Rebbe, since the Rebbe is their replacement.

Therefore, the 18th of Elul—their birthday, on which the powers of the Baal Shem Tov and the Alter Rebbe are strengthened—is a propitious time for strengthening the *hiskashrus* to the Rebbe, the replacement of the Baal Shem Tov and the Alter Rebbe.

**M.** However, the *Yetzer Hara* who is called "the clever one" can come in the guise of humility and argue: The 18th of Elul, the birthday of the Baal Shem Tov, the pillar of light and the pillar of fire—what connection does *you* have to the 18th of Elul?!

"An ordinary Jew"—even when he enters *yechidus* and asks for advice, etc., he does not know the greatness of the Rebbes, what a Rebbe is, and on the other hand, he also holds himself in high regard... and even though he knows that the Rebbe is on a level above him, it is not an incomparable elevation, and he too—he thinks to himself—if only he would exert himself [for he has a good head, and when he sleeps well and wakes up with a clear head, and exerts himself a little], then "you have exerted yourself and found," so that he could reach the Rebbe's level... And as it is explicit in the Sages—he argues—that "*every single one* of Israel is obligated to say, 'When will my deeds reach the deeds of my forefathers Avraham, Yitzchak, and Yaakov?'" And he, who has a good head, *kal v'chomer*!

But he, as a *Chassid*, and especially a *Chabad Chassid*, who knows what a Rebbe is, and on the other hand, knows his own lowliness when performing a *cheshbon hanefesh* for his own sake, for *Chassidim* do not like to deceive themselves...

—[And as is known the story that the students of the Maggid asked the Maggid about a certain matter, where he got it from, and he replied that he heard it from Eliyahu HaNavi, and this answer

**203**

satisfied everyone, except for the Alter Rebbe, who said he wanted proof... and the Maggid said: "This could only be a *Litvak*"...]—

He asks himself: What connection do I have to the 18th of Elul, that I could "take" from this day an increase in Torah and fear of Heaven through strengthening the *hiskashrus*?!

And he continues to argue: Regarding the Baal Shem Tov—it is brought in a *maamar Chassidus* that "the soul of the Baal Shem Tov descended to this world to awaken *Klal Yisrael* from their sleep, that they are in a state of fainting... a remedy for this is that they should whisper his name in his ear, and then he will awaken from the fainting... he whispered in their ears their name, which is called *Yisrael*, etc."

—Such a story happened with the Rebbe, who said that they should whisper his name, the Rebbe's name, in the ear of a woman who had fainted, and thereby she awakened from her fainting. And seemingly, what does the name of a Rebbe in Brooklyn whom she had never seen have to do with that woman?! But it is because the Rebbe's soul is the channel of vitality for *Klal Yisrael*.—

And since the soul of the Baal Shem Tov is the channel of vitality for *Klal Yisrael*—he has a connection to the *Baal Shem Tov*;

But the *Alter Rebbe*—he argues—because he is a *new soul*, unlike all other souls in our time that have already been in a reincarnation in this world (as it is in the writings of the *Arizal*),

## With the help of Heaven. Sicha of Shabbos Parshas Nitzavim, 27 Elul 5710

#### 205

(Unedited)

**1.** It is stated in Likutei Torah (and brought also in the *Reshimos* of the Rebbe Rayatz for Parshas Nitzavim 5707) that "this parsha (Nitzavim) is always read before (on the Shabbos preceding) Rosh Hashanah." Sometimes Nitzavim and Vayelech are read together, and sometimes Nitzavim is read separately, but Parshas Nitzavim is always read on the Shabbos before Rosh Hashanah.

The reason for this is based on the well-known principle that every Shabbos relates to and encompasses the days of the week that follow it. Therefore, on the Shabbos before Rosh Hashanah, we read Parshas Nitzavim, which relates to Rosh Hashanah:

"You are standing *today*" — where "today" refers to Rosh Hashanah, the "day of the great judgment." When "today," the day of Rosh Hashanah, arrives, there must be "you are standing... all of you"—that all souls must stand together "before Hashem your G-d." This matter applies to all souls equally—from "your heads, your tribes" down to "your woodchoppers and your water-drawers," according to Rashi’s interpretation: "that the Canaanites came to convert in the days of Moshe, just as the Gibeonites came in the days of Yehoshua" (who were accepted only because of the oath). Nevertheless, they too ascend before Hashem together with the rest of Bnei Yisrael, in a manner of "*all of you*," "as one, united."

The content of "all of you," "as one, united," is not merely that they tolerate one another despite the fact that one is a "head" and the other is a simple person. Rather, it goes further: they receive from one another and complete one another, just as in the human body the head and the foot complete each other, where neither—not the head nor the foot—has completeness without the other.

And in order for this *avodah* to be possible on Rosh Hashanah, "today," we preface

#### 206

and read in the Torah on the Shabbos before Rosh Hashanah: "You are standing today, all of you, before Hashem your G-d; your heads, your tribes... from your woodchopper to your water-drawer"—for the reading in the Torah constitutes the empowerment to bring these things into actuality.

This is similar to what is explained in Chassidus regarding the reason for reciting the *Malchiyos, Zichronos,* and *Shofaros* verses on Rosh Hashanah—that by bringing *proof from the Torah* that Hashem must be King, that He must remember, and that this is done through the *Shofar*, we cause all of this to be realized in actuality.

**2.** The verse continues (after "You are standing... from your woodchopper to your water-drawer") "to pass into the covenant of Hashem your G-d"—that the *avodah* of "you are standing... all of you" constitutes a preparation "to pass into the covenant," for the forging of the covenant between Hashem and Bnei Yisrael on the day of Rosh Hashanah:

The content of forging a covenant—as we see physically with two loyal lovers who forge a covenant between them that their love shall not cease. Since their love for one another is based on reason—whether because one recognizes the other's virtues, or loves him so that the other will love him back and he will derive benefit from it—then, when he sees a certain deficiency in himself and fears that his friend might notice it, and consequently a weakening in their love will be caused, especially when the deficiency is in essential matters that could cause the total nullification of the love, or even the opposite of love—the advice for this is that when their love is at its peak, they forge a covenant between them that the love will endure forever. For a covenant is above reason and intellect; that is, they set aside their intellect and forge a covenant between them for an essential bond, such that nothing in the world will weaken the love, and it shall be forever and for all eternity.

So too regarding the love between Hashem and Bnei Yisrael: when Rosh Hashanah arrives, at which time the love is at great strength (since they stand after the *avodah* of the month of Elul, which removes the sins that conceal the love), a covenant is forged, whereby Bnei Yisrael bond with Hashem in an essential bond that is above reason and intellect, such that nothing in the world will be capable of weakening it.

And in order to arouse Hashem, as it were, to bond with the souls of Yisrael in a manner above reason and intellect, this is done through the *avodah* of "all of you," "as one, united"—which is the self-surrender that is above reason and intellect [for according to reason, there is no connection or relevance between "your heads" and "your water-drawer"]. Through this, they cause Hashem

#### 207

to bond with the souls of Yisrael in a manner that is above reason and intellect, similar to what is explained regarding the matter of "with all your *me'od* (might)," that through your "might," even though it is yours alone, you draw down the essential "Might."

**3.** However, the matter of "all of you," "as one, united" must be *in truth*. A person must know that this is indeed the reality: although he considers himself one of the "heads" and the other a simple person, besides the fact that it is impossible to know who is the "head" and who is the "foot"—since usually, a person evaluates the other at a lower level than he truly is, while evaluating himself at a higher level than he truly is—beyond that, even if the reality is indeed that he is the "head," there is a virtue in the foot over the head, in that the completeness of the head comes only through the foot, as mentioned above.

Based on this, we can explain the fact that specifically the Gibeonites became woodchoppers for the *Mishkan* and the *Beis Hamikdash*:

Not all Bnei Yisrael were in the *Beis Hamikdash*, and not all lived in Yerushalayim. Only the *Anshei Maamad*, the emissaries of all Yisrael, were there. Yet the work of the Gibeonites was in the *Beis Hamikdash*. The reason for this is that the true completeness of the virtue lies specifically in the foot.

This is also the explanation regarding the connection and relevance to the oath (the oath that was taken at the time of the acceptance of the Gibeonites)—for an oath is above reason and intellect, and reaches the essence of the soul, and the essence of the soul is expressed specifically in the foot.

**4.** When a Jew contemplates that before the *tekiyos* on Rosh Hashanah he will request "He will choose for us our inheritance, the pride of Yaakov whom He loves forever"—where the meaning of "He will choose for us" is "this way and no other"—and contemplates that the matter of choice is specifically from His Essence, may He be blessed, for only He, His Essence, is above all measurements, above all limitations, free; and contemplates also the "incomparability" relative to His Essence, may He be blessed, that more than the incomparability of *Asiyah* relative to *Atzilus*, there is the incomparability of *Atzilus* relative to *Ein Sof*, may He be blessed, that any relationship of "incomparability" that could be described is not a parable at all for the relationship of "incomparability" between the Creator and the created—how does one dare to request from His Essence, may He be blessed, "He will choose for us"?!

When a person contemplates this and evaluates himself, he will have no time to evaluate the other.

— Reb Hillel of Paritch yearned to see the Alter Rebbe, but every time he arrived in a city where the Alter Rebbe had appeared, he did not meet him, because the Alter Rebbe had already left. It occurred to him to hurry and arrive early, and when he heard that the Alter Rebbe was about to arrive at a certain place, he traveled there even before the Alter Rebbe’s arrival. He did not settle for that, for seeing that various obstacles always arose to his seeing the face of the Alter Rebbe, he feared that this time too they would not let him in, so he acted cleverly and came to the apartment where the Alter Rebbe was to stay and hid under the bed.

Reb Hillel prepared a *kushya* in Maseches Arachin in order to debate the *halacha* with the Alter Rebbe. Immediately upon the Alter Rebbe entering the room, even before Reb Hillel could emerge from his hiding place, he heard the Alter Rebbe saying in his well-known *nigun*: "When a *yungerman* has a *kushya* in *Arachin*—he must first evaluate himself," and immediately Reb Hillel fainted. When they found him and revived him from his faint, the Alter Rebbe had already left.

[Reb Hillel never again managed to be by the Alter Rebbe. He would travel to the Mitteler Rebbe and the Tzemach Tzedek, but by the Alter Rebbe, this was the only time he was there, and even then he did not see him.]

We must explain the meaning of this story in *avodas Hashem* regarding us:

*Arachin*—is a matter that is not based on intellect, for the law in *Arachin* is

#### 209

that a person's value is determined by his age, and not by the virtues of the people. All people of the same age have the same value.

Seemingly, someone could raise a *kushya*: I have spent all my years in Torah and *avodah*, in things that Hashem derives pleasure from, that the Heavenly entourage derives pleasure from, and even according to human intellect they are worthy of evaluation, and if so, my years have great value; whereas so-and-so's years were wasted in vain and it would have been better for him not to have been created—how, then, is it possible that our years should have equal value?!

The answer to this: When someone has a *kushya* in *Arachin*—he must first evaluate himself (as is proper), and thereby the *kushya* will be nullified.

***

**5.** Regarding the month of Elul—in addition to the general acronym "*Ani L’dodi V’dodi Li*" (I am to my Beloved and my Beloved is to me), there are also several specific acronyms (which describe the manner of "I am to my Beloved"). In general, they correspond to the three lines: Torah, *Avodah*, and *Gemilas Chasadim*: (a) "*Anah L’yado V’samti Lach*" (I will appoint a place for him)—corresponding to the line of Torah, for words of Torah are absorbent; (b) "*Ish L’re’ehu U’matanos La’evyonim*" (A man to his friend and gifts to the poor)—corresponding to the line of *Gemilas Chasadim* (Tzedakah); (c) "And Hashem your G-d will circumcise *Es L’vavcha V’es L’vav Zar’echa*" (your heart and the heart of your offspring)—corresponding to the line of *Avodah* (Tefillah and Teshuvah in general), as the verse concludes

#### 210

"to love, etc.," and it is explained in *Kuntres HaAvodah* that the essence of the *avodah* of Tefillah is (not fear, but) love.

The matter of Torah and the matter of *Avodah* were explained on another occasion, and now the matter of Tzedakah will be explained—"A man to his friend and gifts to the poor":

Seemingly, it is not understood: since the allusion to Tzedakah is in the words "and gifts to the poor," why were the acronyms of "a man to his friend," which is said regarding "sending portions," also included, and not regarding Tzedakah? And also—on the other hand—why were only the acronyms of (the last words) "a man to his friend" included, and not the acronyms of "sending portions"?

The explanation for this is:

The language is "and gifts *to the poor* (la'evyonim)," not to the needy (*aniyim*)—for the difference between *aniyim* and *evyonim* is that an *ani* is called someone who is not rich, and not even middle-class, but has something, whereas an *evyon* is called someone who "craves everything" (as Rashi explains), that is, craves every small thing ("to every little thing"), because he has nothing.

Furthermore, "craves everything" also applies to the other extreme—that he craves even the greatest thing, because he was accustomed to it, as the Sages say: "even a horse to ride upon," and the like, for since he craves it and without it he is unable to fulfill his mission in the world, you must give it to him.

And since a person might think that he is the *rich man*, and when he gives *to the evyon* a big thing or a small thing, he is giving *from his own*—therefore it says "a man to his friend and gifts to the poor," to teach you that even the "gifts *to the poor*" are in a manner of "a man *to his friend*," that is, that the recipient is *his friend*, and is in the same situation as him (like the giver). One does not need the other, for the giver is not giving from his own; he is only returning to him what

#### 211

is found with him by way of a *deposit*. And as the early Chassidim used to say (in the days of the Alter Rebbe): "The slice of bread that is in my possession is yours just as it is mine" ("yours like mine"), and they would put the word "yours" first. And as the Sages say: "More than the householder does for the poor man, the poor man does for the householder," and likewise the Sages said: "The soul of the poor man... you have kept alive... tomorrow your son or daughter... and I will save them, etc."

**6.** And similarly regarding spiritual Tzedakah—for since a person is composed of body and soul, there is also the matter of Tzedakah in spirituality:

By way of introduction—just as Tzedakah in physicality is an obligation upon every one of Yisrael, even the poor man in Yisrael, so too Tzedakah in spirituality is an obligation upon every one of Yisrael, even the poor man in spirituality; he too is obligated in Tzedakah, for every one of Yisrael has something to give. The very fact that he meets so-and-so constitutes an instruction that he must help and give to so-and-so, for since every matter is by Divine Providence, and Hashem did not create anything in vain, it surely happened with the intention that he should give something to so-and-so.

And just as in physical Tzedakah it is not in a manner that he is the one giving to his friend, but rather that he is doing a favor for himself, as mentioned above, so too it is in spiritual Tzedakah—that through the influence upon the other, he receives from him, for true completeness is only through the inclusion of the head and the foot, as mentioned above.

**7.** Once, the Tzemach Tzedek spoke about the virtue of Tzedakah, how great is the value of assisting a Jew with a livelihood, and how much this helps in *avodas Hashem* for opening the mind and heart to receive the revelations from above.

And the Tzemach Tzedek told his son—the Rebbe Maharash—that it happened to him personally:

When I traveled from Dubrovna to Lubavitch—the Tzemach Tzedek related—I was very satisfied with the inner closeness of the grandfather—the Alter Rebbe—and I hoped that upon my arrival in Lubavitch I would have the merit to see the grandfather with a shining countenance (*in a vision*; this was many years after the passing of the Alter

#### 212

Rebbe). In the meantime, several questions arose for me in matters of *nigleh* and matters of Chassidus, and I organized the matters in my thought.

Upon arriving in Lubavitch, I went immediately to the place about which the grandfather told us on the way to Liadi, that he studied in the synagogue that stood upon it. As I related, the area was then vacant, after a fire that had occurred then.

My father-in-law, the Mitteler Rebbe, related about this place, that fifty-seven years ago, our great Rebbe made Lubavitch a place fit for the leadership of the Chabad Chassidim for long life and years and eternal existence until the coming of Mashiach Tzidkeinu.

From the fact that upon my arrival in Lubavitch the Rebbe ignores me—says the Tzemach Tzedek—I was very broken and fell in my spirit. I had a feeling of a fall, *G-d forbid*, from a high roof to a deep pit. I had prepared myself for inner closeness and in the end I had such distance. I was very saddened, and I searched through my deeds to examine what thing could have caused this distance, in order to do *teshuvah*, so that I would merit to see the holy face of the grandfather and hear matters of Torah and *avodah*.

On Wednesday, 20 Elul, the Tzemach Tzedek went to the synagogue to pray. On his way, he met one of the city's residents named R' Pinchas, who asked him for a *gemilas chasadim* loan in the amount of three rubles, so that he could buy a little at the market and sell and earn for the expenses of Shabbos. The Tzemach Tzedek told him to come to his house after the prayer and then he would give it to him.

At the hour that the Tzemach Tzedek prepared for prayer, and placed his *tallis* on his shoulders, he remembered the words of R' Pinchas that today is market day, and the market opens in the morning and surely R' Pinchas needs the money immediately. The Tzemach Tzedek removed his *tallis*, went home, took five rubles and gave them to R' Pinchas so that he could trade a little and earn.

When the Tzemach Tzedek returned to the *minyan* and washed his hands from the basin to stand in prayer, the Alter Rebbe was revealed to him, and solved his doubts, and his holy face shone.

**8.** We see from this story that Tzedakah acts also in spirituality:

The Tzemach Tzedek—with all his virtues, and especially the closeness he merited during the period of the Alter Rebbe’s passing, when he was alone with the Alter Rebbe.

— The Mitteler Rebbe was in Kremenchug, R' Chaim Avraham was

#### 213

then ill, and R' Moshe remained on the other side of the front and it was not possible for him to come because of the war—

And he had then many instances of closeness from the Alter Rebbe, and in addition to that he did various actions to see the Alter Rebbe—all this did not help.

But when he met a Jew on the street—not within his own four cubits (that is, not that the other came to the Tzemach Tzedek), but within *his* four cubits, of R' Pinchas, and not at the hour that he was saying *Tehillim* and the like, but at the hour that he was thinking how to obtain *gemilas chasadim* so that he could earn—for this he set aside the matter of prayer (and especially the prayer of *the Tzemach Tzedek*), which is a "time of *mochin d'gadlus* above and also below," and its virtue is greater than Torah because through it (through prayer) a change is made *in physicality*, healing the sick and blessing the years (as explained in *Iggeres HaKodesh*), and through this, that he set aside the prayer in order to do a favor for a Jew in physicality—he merited to see the Alter Rebbe.

**9.** Regarding the practical application:

Since yeshiva students have an advantage in two things: (a) they have something to give, since they are found in the four cubits of Torah, (b) they have the virtue of energy, for it is the nature of youth, and therefore they are called by the name "*elem*" from the word for strength—it is worthwhile and proper that they go on Rosh Hashanah to synagogues to arouse Bnei Yisrael.

And since Rosh Hashanah is two days, and each of them has its special virtue: on the first day of Rosh Hashanah there is "harsh judgment," and on the second day "mild judgment," the first day is from the Torah, and the second day is from the Sages, and since the words of the Scribes are strict, each day has an advantage over the other, and they complete each other, until they become "one long day"—they must go to the synagogues on both days.

Someone might argue: since I have a complicated accounting of the whole year, I do not know what stems from the *yetzer hatov* and what from the *yetzer hara*, and now forty days of favor have been given to me

#### 214

so that I can calculate my account—how can I dedicate this time to others? And the answer to this: "A man to his friend and gifts to the poor"—that even "gifts to the poor" are "a man to his friend," and through this, that he will go to arouse his friend—he himself will also be added to.

Each of us wants to see the Rebbe, the Rebbe Rayatz, so that he will solve his questions, for every single person has questions (even one who does not know that he has questions), and in order to solve them one must see the Rebbe. And the advice for this—is going on Rosh Hashanah to synagogues to arouse Bnei Yisrael, souls in bodies, for in this he fulfills the will of the Rebbe, and through this he will merit that the Rebbe will solve his questions in a manner of soul in body.

*

**10.** Regarding going to synagogues—there was a custom in previous times that when they would ask in the synagogue where you are from, they would answer: from Dokshitz and the like, because they were afraid to say that they were coming *from Lubavitch* lest they be expelled... But today, we are in a time where one does not need to be impressed, but one must speak openly: we had and we have a great Rebbe, the Rebbe Rayatz, and on his mission we come!

The truth is that it is impossible to hide the fact of being a "Lubavitcher." Even if he dresses according to the custom of the country in all the refinements (to ensure that the color of the socks is identical to the color of the tie, to take a cigar in his mouth, and to speak fluent English, and so on)—they will recognize and identify him as a "Lubavitcher"... And consequently, it is better to say the truth from the outset.

— The Rebbe Maharash once said to one of the *Maskilim* who was pestering him ("he was pestering him") until the Rebbe Maharash said to him "a sharp word," and the *Maskil* asked him: how do you know, and the Rebbe Maharash answered him: when they bring a Jewish child into the covenant of Avraham Avinu and remove the foreskin from him, there are those who stick the foreskin on their nose ("they put the piece on their nose"), and that is how I recognized—concluded the Rebbe Maharash—on your nose!... And "the measure of good is greater"—that the more he hides, they will recognize and identify that he is a "Lubavitcher."

And the truth is that they would not expel him because of his being a "Lubavitcher"

#### 215

— for "any person who has fear of Heaven, his words are heard"—but rather, because his externality does not match the internality that was placed within him ("the internality that they put into him"), and accordingly they also tell him a reason that is not true ("a false reason"), that they are expelling him because of his being a "Lubavitcher."

We must tell the truth, that we come on the mission of the Rebbe Rayatz, and speak with "breadth," and not be impressed by anyone, similar to the parable (brought in Chassidus) of a great wise man that those who are not wise are not considered in his eyes at all...—one must indeed be careful not to hurt anyone ("not to tear off the other's nose"), and "the words of the wise are heard in tranquility," but, together with this, one must go with "breadth," and not be impressed by anyone.

— When they printed the "Toras Shmuel," there were those who came with complaints that they were printing in cursive letters (rounded), and not in square letters, which is easier (and especially in America) to read. Since the complaints reached me—as I was the one bringing it to print—I entered to the Rebbe Rayatz and told him that there are those who complain that besides the effort on the content of the matters they will have to exert themselves on reading the script, and the Rebbe Rayatz answered with a parable: it is enough to drag the "trough" to the horses, the time has come to drag the horses to the "trough"!

The difference between the two ways is that when they drag the horse to the trough, it is against the will of the horse, who thinks that they want to make him "no longer a horse," or at least make him a little less "a horse" than he was before, and therefore he screams that his desire is to remain a "horse" with all his might, and therefore the advice is to drag the trough to the horse, for then, at the time of the action, he does not feel that the intention is to make him "no longer a horse," and afterwards, he will no longer care about being "no longer a horse"...

And nevertheless, the time has already come—as the words of the Rebbe Rayatz—not to be impressed by the horse, but to drag him to the trough and make him "no longer a horse."

***

#### 216

**11.** At the end of the *maamar* of the three *Selichos*, the Rebbe Rayatz brings the parable of the "King in the field" explained in Likutei Torah, but with slight changes, and this is its language:

"And the parable for this is that the King, before his arrival in the city, the people of the city go out to meet him and receive his face in the field, and then all who wish are permitted and able to go out to meet his face, and the King receives everyone with a pleasant countenance and shows a smiling face to everyone, and upon his going to the city, behold, everyone follows him; however, upon his arrival at the royal palace, they do not enter to the King except by way of great preparation, and with permission, and even this only the chosen of the people and the select few."

The changes from the language in *Likkutei Torah* are: (a) In *Likkutei Torah* it is said that "then *they are permitted* all who wish, etc.," and the Rebbe Rayatz adds "*they are permitted and able*, etc." (b) In *Likkutei Torah* it is said "and upon his going to the city, behold *they* follow him," and the Rebbe Rayatz writes that "*everyone* follows him." (c) In *Likkutei Torah* it is said that "upon his arrival at the royal palace, they do not enter except *with permission*, etc.," and the Rebbe Rayatz adds "by way of *great preparation* and with permission, etc."

And one must understand the reason for the change in language—for seemingly, either way, since he is not writing his own language, but using the language that is in *Likkutei Torah*, why change the language that is in *Likkutei Torah* with the above changes?

— The Rebbe Rayatz once said that there is a difference between saying a *maamar* that he has internalized ("that with this *maamar* he will catch a Jew"...) and saying a *maamar* that he has not internalized (that even though he also said this *maamar*, there was not in it the matter of "catching a Jew"). And in the present case, the changes from the language that is in *Likkutei Torah* emphasize the internalization, and consequently, one must understand what is his intention in these changes.

**12.** And it can be said that the reason for the changes is in accordance with the change in the status and situation from the generation of the Alter Rebbe to our generation—that in the time of the Alter Rebbe there was no need to clarify ("to warn") so much, unlike in our generation.

— One of the Chassidim who was in the time of the Alter Rebbe and afterwards in the time of the Mitteler Rebbe and the Tzemach Tzedek, said, that once they used to say "short Torahs" [for even the long *maamarim* of the Alter Rebbe are "in short" relative to

#### 217

the length of the *maamarim* in the generations that followed] that would perform their action ("it would penetrate"), unlike today... And if the things are said regarding that time, in our days all the more so!

And the explanation for this:

Regarding the first change—in the generation of the Alter Rebbe it was enough to say that "*they are permitted* all who wish to go out and meet his face," and there was no need to add more, unlike in our generation where we must add explicitly that "*they are permitted and able, etc.* to meet his face."

And similarly regarding the second change—that in the generation of the Alter Rebbe it is enough to say that "upon his going to the city, behold *they* follow him," and it is understood by itself that "they" refers to those about whom it is spoken beforehand; unlike for our generation—the generation in which the Rebbe Rayatz lived ("lived and lives")—he added in the *maamar* that he sent to all the expanses of the world that "*everyone* follows him," in order to clarify clearly (so that there should be no room for error) that the intention is to everyone literally, even those to whom the King showed a smiling face and it did not help them, even they—everyone—follows him.

And regarding the third change—the condition of "*great preparation*" in addition to the "permission," which seemingly is the opposite of the good ("is the Rebbe not a good one, *G-d forbid*, that he adds the condition of great preparation?!")—it can be said that this is giving advice for receiving the permission:

Even though the entire existence of the ministers (who are appointed to give the permission) is nothing but their being ministers *of the King*, nevertheless, when they come to authority and one must ask them for permission, it is possible that they will not want to give permission.

And therefore the Rebbe Rayatz adds the matter of "great preparation," for this preparation—the content of which is to cherish the time, every moment, and to fill it with letters of Torah and Tefillah—*belongs to everyone*, and consequently he can argue: since the Rebbe commanded me to do this preparation, it is impossible that this preparation should go to waste...

— In addition to the fact that through this he will be added to with many matters of Torah and Mitzvos, and it is known that the angel Michael gives everything ("gives everything") for a *mitzvah*—

And through this he will be given the permission to enter the King's palace.

#### 218

**13.** And it should be added and explained another slight change—that in *Likkutei Torah* the language is "upon his arrival at the royal palace (*malchuso*)," whereas the Rebbe Rayatz writes "upon his arrival at the royal palace (*malchus*)" (without the additional *vav*).

And it can be said that the intention of the Rebbe Rayatz is to add that the entry is to such a place where it is not relevant to note ("to mark") his kingdom (but rather kingdom in general), for it is above all signs...

Similar to what is explained in *Likkutei Torah* in the interpretation of the verse "for on this day He will atone for you"—the reason that it is not specified in the verse who is the one atoning, but rather "He will atone" in general.

**14.** At the end of the *maamar*, the Rebbe Rayatz writes that "through the arousal of the point of the heart in *teshuvah* from the depths of the heart, one becomes a pure vessel to receive the abundance of blessing of Rosh Hashanah for the whole year."

(The Rebbe wept and said:) And the main blessing—that the Rebbe should take us out of the physical exile and out of the spiritual exile, and lead us toward the true and complete Redemption.

***

## B"H, First Day of Selichos, 5711

#### 219

To the students of the Yeshivos,
May Hashem protect them and grant them life,

Peace and blessing!

With the permission of the esteemed Rabbis, the administrators of the Yeshivos, I am writing to you, my friends, the students of the Yeshivos, in particular, in addition to my letter of 18 Elul.

The great good fortune that Divine Providence has granted you to be among those whose portion is the Torah of Hashem, and the special quality that Hashem has imbued in the youth—that they possess the greatest strength and energy—these two things place upon you a special responsibility to be among the first, both in doing and in causing others to do, to provide spiritual charity to those who are "poor in knowledge," and to give it with a generous eye and an open hand.

Our Sages have ruled: The positive mitzvah of Torah study is, in and of itself, greater than all other mitzvos. It is equal to them all because study leads to action. However, if a mitzvah cannot be performed by others—for instance, if one’s words are more likely to be heeded, or if the actions of others are insufficient for this mitzvah—one interrupts one’s study to perform the mitzvah, and only afterwards returns to one’s study.

During the days of the month of Tishrei that is approaching, the month in which it is the duty and privilege of every person in Israel to "renew his deeds and improve his deeds," you, the students of the Yeshivos, must gird yourselves with greater strength and intensity:

(a) In your avodah with yourselves: To awaken with a true awakening of teshuvah, an acceptance of the yoke of His sovereignty, may He be blessed, and an acceptance of the yoke of Torah with yiras shamayim and the performance of mitzvos with joy.

#### 220

(b) In your avodah with others: To bring close those who need to be brought close, and to strengthen those who need to be strengthened—in the avodah of teshuvah, the study of Torah, and the performance of mitzvos. And in particular, to conduct a broad campaign, in all appropriate ways, so that every Jewish boy and girl, young man and young woman, is educated with a proper education, in the spirit of our ancestors, without compromises.

"And the Holy One, blessed be He, said to Israel: If you improve your deeds, I will rise from the throne of judgment and sit upon the throne of mercy, and I will transform the attribute of judgment into the attribute of mercy for you."

May you be blessed and be a source of blessing with a *Kesivah Vachasimah Tovah*.

**Menachem Mendel Schneerson**
Son-in-law of the Nasi, our holy master,
our teacher and Rebbe (of Lubavitch), may the merit of the righteous protect us.

## 221

It is well known, as explained in many places in Chassidus[1](#_ftn1079), why this day is called *Rosh Hashanah* (Head of the Year) and not *Techilas Hashanah* (Beginning of the Year). It is because this day relates to the rest of the days of the year just as the head relates to the body and its limbs. Just as the head contains the vitality of all the limbs—and subsequently, in a revealed manner, the vitality for each individual limb is drawn from the head[2](#_ftn1080)—so too, the day of *Rosh Hashanah* contains within it the vitality of all the days of the year. All of them are included within it in a concealed state, and are subsequently distributed in a revealed manner to the individual days.

It can be said that this is one of the reasons for the warning we have received to cherish the time during *Rosh Hashanah* above all else. The hours and moments of these days are general and all-encompassing, and they are superior to the rest of the days of the year—not only in quantity, but also in quality, similar to the substance of the brain in the head compared to the substance of the other limbs of the body.

It is understood that just as with the head and the limbs of the body—when is a person healthy? When the vitality in the head is in its state of perfection, and that vitality is also drawn from it to each and every limb according to what is appropriate for it—so too is the *avodah* of the days of *Rosh Hashanah*. These days must be appropriate to their nature: the head of the entire year. Furthermore, they must be connected to the rest of the days of the year, to influence them with the vitality of accepting the yoke of His Kingship, may He be blessed, the study of Torah, and the fulfillment of *mitzvos*.

***

Now, among the matters that assist in *avodah* in general, and in the *avodah* of connecting *Rosh Hashanah* with the rest of the days of the year in particular, is the connection with the "Head of the thousands of Yisroel," whose *neshamah*[3](#_ftn1081) is the aspect of the head and the *mochin* (intellectual faculties) for the rest of the *neshamos* of his generation. From him is their nourishment and their vitality, and through him they are connected and unified in their primary essence and their root.

#### 222

Likewise, for each and every one of us, the connection with the *Nasi* and head of our generation—our master, teacher, and Rebbe, the Rayatz, of blessed memory—is the key. He has already explained that this connection is through studying his Torah and walking in the straight path that he has taught us, to follow in his ways. This is what will assist in the *avodah* of the days of *Rosh Hashanah*[4](#_ftn1082) and their connection with the rest of the days of the year.

And it will be the channel through which a *kesivah v’chasimah tovah* (a good inscription and sealing) will be drawn—in goodness that is seen and revealed, in both material and spiritual matters—during the days of *Rosh Hashanah* and subsequently throughout the entire year.

**Menachem Mendel son of Chana Schneerson**

*First day of Selichos, 5710*
*Brooklyn, N.Y.*

## B’s”d. R”D, Monday, Parshas Acharei-Kedoshim, 7 Iyar, 5710

#### 225

[In response to the fact that there are changes, etc., in spiritual avodah – the Rebbe said:]

If there are changes Above, should there not be changes below?!

This is the shortcoming of many bochurim: they think about themselves too much. One must think about others, at least one hour every day—for this was one of the Rebbe’s demands in his sichos, letters, etc.: to engage with others ("ton mit yenem"). The Rebbe took you upon his shoulders ("aych hot der Rebbe genumen af zayne pleytzes"), but you must engage, and engage on your own ("m'darf ton, ton aleyn").

When a thought about oneself arises, and one sees that it distracts from avodah, increases melancholy, etc.—one must push it away. And if one has nothing with which to push away the melancholy because one’s situation is not as it should be—one should remind oneself of the necessity to occupy oneself with the good of another, and to think about the other: how to be of benefit to him.

There must be hisbonenus, especially during davening, since this is what the Rebbe wrote to *him* in his letter, and one should connect the hisbonenus with the meaning of the words one is davening. For example, in Pesukei D’zimra—since he is currently studying the continuation of *Maschini* 5701[1](#_ftn1083), where the verse[2](#_ftn1084) "Praise Hashem from the heavens" is explained. And in the blessings of Krias Shema, there must be the proper focus [histaklus][3](#_ftn1085).

The hisbonenus in davening does not need to be an entire maamar at once; one point is sufficient. And even if, on the face of it, it seems unrelated—the hisbonenus on the meaning of the words, just as one learned before davening—this is not considered an interruption [hefsek]. I once asked the Rebbe about this: is this not similar to a "foreign thought" [machshava zarah]—for "zarah" means foreign to that place—and he answered that it is not so[4](#_ftn1086). And this itself must be done in those places where it is permitted according to nigleh to interrupt, so that one does not take so much time that he finishes the entire thing, etc.

#### 226

He explained in what manner one must study Chassidus in order to understand it well, and brought an example from *Chazakas HaBatim*.

He said: The Rebbe gave you work for your entire life ("aych hot der Rebbe gegebn arbet oyfn gantzn lebn").

When you engage with others—this itself will help you in your hisbonenus. As the Alter Rebbe says[5](#_ftn1087): his mind and heart become a thousand times more refined[6](#_ftn1088). From this we learn two things: that even so, one must engage in hisbonenus, because the mind and heart, even though they are refined many times over, still require action [to be applied] within them[7](#_ftn1089); and that when one engages in hisbonenus, one attains [understanding] more easily and with greater depth, and accomplishes more.

An additional promise from the Rebbe: that when you do all these things, then—as the Rebbe promises in several of his sichos—you will succeed in both gashmius and ruchnius.

## With the help of Heaven. Resh-Dalet, Monday, Parshas Ki Savo, 15 Elul 5710

#### 227

(Unedited)

There is a letter from the Rebbe to Jewish soldiers, in which he writes that in a person’s life there are various events that one must pass through—for example, military service and the like—and one must know that these events are only temporary ("zeit-veilike zachen"), and afterwards they pass, whereas the matters of Torah and mitzvos are eternal, remaining permanently.

A Jew must always be connected to the Ribono shel Olam—by fulfilling the matters of Torah and mitzvos that the Ribono shel Olam has given us.

In his letter, the Rebbe suggests that every Jewish soldier should recite a kapitel Tehillim every day.

Therefore: In addition to the fact that you will certainly be careful to put on tefillin, you should also strive to say Tehillim, to learn a possuk of Chumash with Rashi’s commentary, and to read maamarim on matters of emunah (in whichever language is easier for you).

And through this, the Ribono shel Olam will protect you in every place of danger, and will return you in peace, healthy, strong, and whole.

And as we stand in the month of Elul, I wish you a *kesivah v’chasimah tovah*.

[Before his departure, the Rebbe instructed him to write to him from the place where he would be stationed in the army.]
